O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the last of the science of medicine,  by its completion I have reached its end, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me that I should fulfill my duty to you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation with the hand, that is, surgery.
Because the operation with the hand is almost completely absent in our region and in our time, to the point that the knowledge of it is greatly diminished and its vestiges are lost; and there remains of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly altered and have led to errors and hesitation, so that their intentions are obscured and their strength and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it by the ordering of this treatise, following the path of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and so that it comes with the forms of the cautery irons and other instruments of the work, as may be by addition of the explanation and by preparation for it, which is a necessary matter.
But the reason why a skilled artisan with his hand is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which Galen mastered, so that he knows the arrangement of the members and their forms, and their connection and separation; and he should have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that with one name much is said, but little in the work, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable of what we have said about anatomy is not exempt from falling into error, by which men will die, as I have seen many of them, because some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who had grown old, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened that a black slave who had a fracture in his thigh came to him; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, above the wound, with cushions and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound to breathe [to heal properly].
And then he unbound it according to his desires, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for some days and ordered that we not unbind the bandage; and it remained so until the thigh and foot became suppurated, and it came to ruin/destruction [the leg was lost].
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for several days, until the malignancy of it was magnified by its lord [the condition worsened significantly].
The reason is that a hot cancer is pure melancholic humor; therefore, it should not be approached with iron at all, but should be in a limb which suffers that it is completely stopped/obstructed.
And for that reason, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be divided into two parts: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a work in which there is danger in the most favorable conditions.
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where operations occur that involve error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to observe this and to avoid that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and avoid terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and keep your instruments from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not overcome you: for it is more conducive to your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called a bad physician.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet, the forms of instruments and the tools of cauterization, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I speak of the benefits of this cautery and of its harms, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the benefits of cautery and its harms is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in its science, and secretly hidden; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discourse suffices for me, I fear prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are cold complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable for an illness which is characterized by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry illness should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I refute him who says the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry illness that occurs in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of a man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, in support of this argument, that experience has revealed this to me on several occasions.
However, it is not fitting that this should come from someone who has already been exhausted and exercised in the chapter on cautery through previous practice, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of illnesses within themselves, and the causes of those illnesses, and their accidents, and the span of time of those illnesses.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, for all physicians have agreed on those, and are not in disagreement about the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness, is superior to cautery with a burning medicine, because fire is simple, its action does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized, and does not harm another member adjacent to it except with slight harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches even those members which are distant from it, and easily brings about in the member an illness difficult to cure and easily causes death.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except that it flows away. And it is known to us through experiment and long service of study in the art concerning the truth of the causes; and therefore I am excused from the length of the discourse. And if it were not that it is not permissible to reveal to you in this brief writing my secret concerning fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies, and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with a philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be difficult for your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they consider springtime the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and urgent, which do not tolerate delay, because it is feared that greater harm will come than the slight harm introduced by the time.
And this does not occur to you, O sons, this is not considered except by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is, that for that illness which is healed by cautery there is no remission at all times, and they assume that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for that reason, because cautery is nothing other than a kind of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
But however, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the speed of its effectiveness and the power of its operation and the greatness of its lord (or master).
And it is possible that his illness returns at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has reached him in his acquisition of food and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that that pain does not return to him, but this happens rarely.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a true saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine or with anything besides cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the process, one administers cautery, and it benefits that condition. And therefore from here it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the preciousness of gold and the excellence of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although this is not entirely true, for I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies but not others, and cautery with it is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you want due to the hardness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply fire to it, the gold melts from the fire, and some flakes fall into the fire so that the artisan is bothered by this.
And for this reason, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the right operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head down to the feet, so that I may easily give the enquirer what he wants.
The first chapter.
This cauterization counteracts the humidity and coldness affecting the brain, the accidents of which are twofold:  a cause of excessive and profuse rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears; much sleepiness; toothache; throat pain; and universally all illnesses that come from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden illness, apoplexy, and similar diseases.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with a purgative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and constitution.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a low bench and place his hands on his feet.
Then place the root of your palm against the area above the root of the nose, between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that place, mark the spot with a pen, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
Then place it on the spot marked with the pen, in such a way that it descends, your hand being gradually tightened, and you turn it.
Then lift your hand slightly and observe the area, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the size of a grain of barley, then raise your hand; and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another of them if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then place on it cotton soaked in wine and leave it there until the burn scab is destroyed. Then treat the area with tetrafarmicon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, it is better to leave the arm that way, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the elbow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's surface is hardened/burned.
Then scrape away what has been burned from the bone, and then let it be treated/healed.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the back, until it first deeply penetrates the bone, until the bone is charred, and this is the method for a small alphecat (likely referring to a specific type of wound or ulcer): And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then let it be healed until it is consolidated/closed.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a way of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health when it is; because the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a fragile nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and use that and work with that for health.
Chapter Two.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the whole head and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and head purges and plasters and poultices and cauteries and ointments and the like, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we have recounted and none of those things benefits him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not fragile and finds much great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is parted. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vessel — and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much great pain, different from the pain of all other cauterizations of the head.
And I would say this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter Three.
Concerning the cauterization of recent (or non-chronic) migraine.
When a sharp pain occurs in the part of the head, or a spreading pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on diseases; and if all this does not help, then cauterization there is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off the ends from both sides. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made there under the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress for continuous constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then unbind it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place on it cotton soaked in vinegar until the place becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner: ignite the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called *claus clavilhs* (likely a description of a specific cautery tool), because its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is a certain curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the burning, which is below, is not hardened — and rotate the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of what is burned by the specific heat is about half of it, and lift your hand so that the artery which is beneath is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the location, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton moistened with butter, and then cure with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a surgical instrument like a cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this specific, not-ancient, type of headache (migraine).
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not help, and sometimes the illness is not sufficiently addressed by what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then it is necessary that you heat the cauterizing knife until it is white-hot, then mark the painful area with a line whose length is half a finger, or thereabouts; and place your hand for a moment and press until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle is not injured, and the nerve that controls it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, for in its incision there is danger, and especially to him who ignores what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this illness is not relieved by this cautery and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize that in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of the artery with a cautery having two blades; this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear occurs due to cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatment that I have described in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery immediately.  This is the one called "puncta," of which this is the form:
And then apply the puncture, then the cauterization, and let it be carried around the whole ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in a specific part. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the spot with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten punctures in the whole ear, or in whichever part, then afterwards treat the spots until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of a sprain.
A sprain which is cured with cauterization is only one of the types which arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or from spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of sprain with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries:  one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening (or alleviation) only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the ear, a little above the temple, and another in the temple; the length of which should be according to the length of the thumb, and hold your hand with the cautery until the amount of half the thickness of the fingernail is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of cutlery, the form of which is shown above/before. (This implies a diagram or illustration was present in the original text.) However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some of the superfluous thickness.
And then care for the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your medication is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize them with four cauteries: on all the corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is further back; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Of cauterization for forgetfulness, done by flame.
So that large enemas and purifying pills are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to their neck a plaster of the kind described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already mentioned.
And if he is healed by these means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations on the back part of his head, which descend from the uppermost part of the head to the lowermost part of the neck. And place between each cauterization the width of a finger, and then care for the cauterization with what has been prescribed.
But if you add to it and the patient tolerates it, then make a cauterization in the middle place; and if you want a greater addition [or stronger effect], then cauterize that over the two temples.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
As you proceed with the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery over both temples of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries over the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and lastly with a cautery until it is numb from the burning, and raise your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the purging of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on his occiput, and cauterize on each temple of his head. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division [of treatments].
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made poultice for him, and apply that poultice to the middle of his head.
And the patient should be seated and held firmly on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most fitting to use old sheep's fat. And heat that [liniment/ointment] over the fire with moderate warmth, the amount of which the said sufferer can bear when it is applied to him. Then pour that [liniment/ointment] onto the middle of his head within the circle [previously made], and leave it there until it cools.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other good treatments, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize that area by making small punctures according to his need, but you should hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a kind of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in sheep's fat or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the onset of water in the eye is evident to you through the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient a drink that will purify his head, and keep it free of all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the onset of the flow of water is in both eyes, or on one side if the onset of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the occiput; and let the cauteries be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is possible for you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed apart.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (or, Concerning the cauterization of chronic ulcers/sores).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the first chapter concerning water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it requires additional treatment, then cauterize that in all parts of the corner of the eye above the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or, Concerning the cauterization of a foul-smelling nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient decoctions to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauterizations above the two eyebrows, slightly below the hairline.
And beware of burning too deeply, that you do not incinerate it (or, that you do not damage it excessively).
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form/shape.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for the softening/relaxation of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or by humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that, [do it] above the two eyebrows a little, with two cauteries in each part, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery, but only so that a third part of the eyelid is hardened/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in such cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a corrosive medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave their eyelashes as if they were cleaning them, until they are long and strong; and if they prick the eyelid at their root, then you should bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not hurt until the eyelashes grow back.
Then, when they have grown and strengthened, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of their eye with a probe, a mark that is in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then apply lint soaked in egg white or in gum arabic to the eye.
And then heat the cautery, whose form is this.
And then cauterize over the shape you have marked, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs around it raised from the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then apply lint with a bandage until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the skin near its origin and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not touch the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this is to instruct the patient to pluck the hairs until they are lengthened and evened, and then a myrtle leaf-shaped bandage of cloth should be applied.
Then take known soap and quicklime, about an ounce, or whatever, and mix these ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; then spread this mixture on the cloth which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this on one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it slightly, but do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like that of fire.
And so when you perceive that he feels the bite, then release the medicine and rub it with your finger; when the bite ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; then consider if you see that the eyelid is raised as it should be after being cauterized by fire or incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet been applied and does not blacken until your work is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is eradicated, and then treat it with healing ointment or another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to guard with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid is softened after a few days and some of the hair-like matter remains in the eye, then repeat your medicine on the place which is softened in the eyelid, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and then cover it until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaves.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore your cauterization is done accordingly.
And the path of rectitude shall not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is ineffective, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and let a minister hold the patient's head between your hands in such a way that their head is not moved or shaken.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or gum arabic on their eye.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is as follows:
which should be concave, like the shape of a cane or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place on it the cautery, which should be well ignited, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not err or the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the granulation of the eye and destroy it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place on it a cotton ball with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then place on it a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered and scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancient physicians described:  Find the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin, slender tube which fits its form. And pour into it one ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with a cloth with good retention, and do not disturb the patient so that the melted lead does not reach the patient's eye; and it is necessary that you place on the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the location of the fistula and heal it by a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal probe and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it should return to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of the fissure of the well (likely referring to a body orifice or wound).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and mostly on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the sections and your treatment is not effective, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the subtlety of a knife.
Then carefully place that instrument in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat that with cerots until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops, then breaks, becomes chronic, and pus flows, forming a fistula, and if you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula.  Then place the heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom, and do this once or twice at the back; then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so, if the wound is closed and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to open the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines without success, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described later, in such a way that you place the small cup over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, holding your hand briefly until the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cautery a few times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried out.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or worms in it, and you don't achieve a cure with medicine, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a cow's hair cautery, or cauterization with fire.
Cautery with hair is that you take cow's hair and boil it in an iron spoon, and then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, and then dip it in the boiled hair, and apply it to the aching tooth quickly until it is cooled.
And then repeat this several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned hair, and place that on the aching tooth, and place a glowing iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you use a small tube of brass or iron, and there should be some thickness in its body so the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired temperature, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be lessened either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, by the application of this cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good wool and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophulos (likely referring to a type of skin eruption or swelling).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them lightly, then you ignite the cautery, the shape of which is as follows: having two open ends so that the smoke escapes from one end of the cautery, and place this ignited instrument on the abscess once, and again if it is needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cloth soaked in boiled water over it until that which the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages and cloths until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humidity comes upon the lung's channel, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and then cauterize that area below the root of the throat in the affected location.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn the uvula, but only a little.
And cauterize that area with another cautery near the juncture of the neck at the back of his last vertebra. And his cautery should be a small nail, following the previous manner.
And then treat that with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
On the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption (tuberculosis) and the disease is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the previously mentioned places – the left and right sides – and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a small nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, let your cauterization be done with points using the cautery that is called a puncta (puncture), the form of which is described in the section on earache puncturing.
And let the points be placed close to those; and then treat it with the same things as are prescribed before until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this method:
Chapter 25.
Concerning the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the spermatic cord.
When the head of the spermatic cord is dislocated because of lubricous humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is in its accustomed place of return, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again as it happened, as we have testified, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation; and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then raise the cord which is in the inner part of the testicle to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated in the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the cord until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the cauterization will then have the form of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place on the cautery cooked porridge with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened, the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying, and if you continue the treatments and do not help, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his arms and legs, and then cauterize him with three cauteries: one cautery under the feet, the amount of one finger, using a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on both sides of the cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauteries is created;  and space them so they do not touch, lest they cause putrefaction.
And if the depth of the cautery is two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the shape of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the form of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized punctually where this cautery is feared: that is, you mark points on the stomach with a probe according to the amount you want, and then cauterize those points; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
Of the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, humidity, or excessive flatulence, such that it is thrown out of its normal fullness by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then the patient should be laid supine on his neck, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same amount on the liver under the hypochondria, where the decubitus of a man is perceived.
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the amount of what is burned is the amount of half the width of the little finger, and no more should be applied; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him lie down, having already spread his thighs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-shaped cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the width of a whole finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and it reaches the intestines, for the finger here is slender; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When an ulcer or abscess develops in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very sharp and sometimes it fatigues the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto his side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then heat the cautery, which is shaped like a ploughshare, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the pus is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until healthy blood comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this method of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated;  but this, with some experience, should be attempted in this operation, yet leave this to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery of the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this method, is effective, and it is this: that you take of the roots of long, dry birthwort a root one finger-length long which is found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse that in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with one cautery in the place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly inclined towards the part which is below the beard, and with two cauteries in that which is inclined between the fifth rib and the sixth, inclining slightly towards the back parts; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another above the stomach; and with three cauteries of the back parts; and with one cautery in the place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp your hand with the cautery, but rather let it be in the manifest handle in work.  (A more natural English translation might be: "And you should not hold the cautery with your hand, but rather use the handle provided.")
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and ignite it, and put it in the place which is between the ribs, until it reaches that abscess and is purged healthy, as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with cautery is not without danger, for one may quickly kill the tissue in the place, creating a fistula which is not easily healed.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said of treatment in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to make three or four cauteries lengthwise according to the length of the spleen, following the pattern of the liver cauteries, the form of which has been previously described.
And let the width between each cautery be the breadth of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and let the patient be lying on his back.
And another way of cauterizing is to ignite the cautery having two points, which method we described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the rib which is over the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your elevation, with which you raise the rib, be according to the length of the body, so that cauteries are made according to the width of the body.
Then insert the two points, very hot, into that space so that with them you penetrate the rib on the other side.
And then extract the cautery; and thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you treat the cautery until it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding causes in the cure.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cautery is not applied in dropsy, except in a certain [specific, particular] case.  (The "3" likely indicates a footnote or marginal note in the original, which is impossible to translate without seeing the original manuscript).
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you cauterize them with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery above the stomach, and with another cautery above the liver, and with another cautery above the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery above the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be proportionate to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cautery open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the cautery from the patient before it is necessary, because two intentions should be combined, and healing should be hastened with help.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above, and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described above.
Chapter 32.
On the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are afflicted with dropsy and are filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the back of the foot, in the space between the little toe and the ring finger. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; and then raise your hand, and do not completely remove it, for the yellowish water will drain.
The cautery is according to this shape:  Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh a cautery should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other in the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water drains from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp to the point that it weakens the vital functions, that is, the digestive and retentive functions, which are in the stomach and intestines, and this is treated with methods of healing and doesn't improve, and sometimes the patient suffers and needs cauterization to stimulate the vital forces, then cauterize it with a large cautery on the stomach, as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in a circle around the navel, with a thin, pointed cautery; and with a cautery on the flank, on a large aloes, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it on the groin and on the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries near the stomach and near the inguinal regions, for this is a perfect and beneficial cure of which you will not be mistaken.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are chronic hemorrhoids in the nature of thick, cold humors or corrupted moistures, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and the treatment does not improve, then cauterize with three small, triangular cauteries just below the spine of the two [presumably, referring to the sacrum/coccyx area], and with a cautery above the navel, done in two applications.
And if you suspect that the patient's stomach is cold and their food is not digested, and you see their face is inflamed, then cauterize the stomach with a large cauterizing iron as previously described; and with one cautery iron on the liver, and with another cautery iron on the spleen—using a nail-shaped cauterizing iron—and leave the cauterization open for some time, and then treat the patient until they are healed.
Chapter 35.
Concerning the cauterization of an abscess, near its excision.
When you excise an abscess, then you immediately apply a cautery that resembles a ray (or spoke). Then insert that heated cautery into the excised abscess, and lastly place your hand there until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
And do this once or twice, for if the abscesses are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is slower in helping.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36.  On the cauterization of fistulas that are formed naturally in the body's parts.
When nature does not heal the ailment which we have described in its place, and the patient is frightened by it, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the natural area of the body, and it is old,  it will be flowing with pus and corrupted humors. Therefore, what is most appropriate is to probe it with a thin probe, and then know the quantity and depth of it with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which is similar to a ray (or, a pointed cautery). And then place that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the depth indicated by the probe.
And repeatedly apply the cautery until those corrupted tissues are burned, once or twice or three times, depending on your assessment of the need.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or the intestine, then beware of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you believe it is not penetrating deeply.
And then care for the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the matter has been excised from it, and remains so for a long time, then know that it is completely cured.
And if the matter has not been removed, then know that it is penetrating or in the bone itself that is corrupted, or that it comes from those causes, which will be recalled in their proper place (in the later part of the text).
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of ulcers.
When in ulcers pain arises from cold or excessive inflammation, and because of this the patient's body is weakened, then it is necessary to cauterize that over the ulcers with a cautery, on each ulcer with a nail-shaped cautery, the preparation of which will be recalled later.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations result, and thus they will be more helpful in the healing.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left side.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped instrument, as is appropriate.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the hand.
When cold and dampness come to the hand, and because of this conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their onset is delayed, and pain occurs at their arrival, then it is necessary that the place be cauterized with three cauteries in the circuit of the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery above the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient over the area of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the area as it is dislocated, so that the area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described for the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be made according to the size of the thickness of the entire bone; and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and do not disagree, and this is ancient practice, then you must purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or artificial pills or similar; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Now, according to two known methods, one of which is to cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the width of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the whole hip.
And this is the manner of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as the location of the pain; and with one cautery on the buttock, four fingers slightly below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain extends to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery for three or four points, or more if necessary.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not go so deep with the cautery that you burn nerves or cause great injury, and that because of this you do not cause harm or contraction to the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg became swollen until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise ones say that in the cauterization of an ankle, a cautery of this kind is used:  make a circle of iron whose circumference is half a palm; and let its opening be according to the thickness of a date pit or slightly less.
And within that circle let there be another, a third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazel nut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a hazelnut or two, and make in those iron margins decently prepared in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave that for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for several days; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Albucasis says: O, son, we have used this type of cautery little because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who dare to look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and find with that its place. (This translates more literally than perfectly. A better, more natural-sounding translation might be: "But such cauteries are good for those who endure them, and thus one finds the affected area.")
But cauterization with burning medicines is that you make two circles having a perforated handle in which you insert the  iron door latch; let the length of the walls in the height be two nuts or whatever those are. (This is a difficult sentence due to the archaic language and possibly some technical terms that aren't completely clear.  The translation attempts to capture the meaning but some ambiguity remains regarding the description of the instrument.)
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of the finger; and let them be opened below and above, and let them contain themselves between each other according to this form: (This describes a surgical procedure, likely involving incisions. The "form" is left undefined, requiring further context from the surrounding text or a diagram.)
Then place the patient on their side, over the affected part of the hip, and press your hand on the area of the hip; and then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and let the heat be gentle and gradual, and hold it for about an hour, and let the patient endure the biting and burning — for this produces a burning sensation like fire — until the burning ceases.
Then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the entire hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and apply a bandage until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for a few days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, apply a similar water bath as was done for the hip.
And this is the method of the warm water treatment, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and put them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated with a small hole, according to the amount that should go into the ray.  (Note: "ray" here likely refers to a specific instrument or device, the exact nature of which is unclear from this sentence alone).
And place another inverted pot below the bottom of the pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water that covers them by a finger's breadth, and then when you have pressed them with your hand with good compression, allow the sharp water to descend from above into the inverted pot below.
Then collect all that water, and pour it over fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be a very strong solution, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterization of other members, because it acts like fire.
And some of the medicines with which cauterization is also done are:  honey,  vinegar, and quicklime with soap, so that they are mixed together.
And Gualia recounted from an ancient man the operation and cure by which pain of the hip and sciatic vein is cured, and greatly praised the effectiveness of it, saying that no other cure was needed, and that it was healed in one go as recommended, because very quickly that person entered the bath and was immediately free of it.
And it is so that [some] green hellebore should be taken, and if green is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little grease and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels a burning sensation.
Then put that person in a bath, and when their body is reddened, expose them to the sun, for the pain ceases and they are healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
However, Dioscorides says that when the varicose vein is cauterized with a goat's horn, it will greatly benefit him; and the cautery should be applied in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the arches of the foot and the ankle bone.
Then take a dry goat's horn and burn it in a fire until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of the burning reaches the ankle and the pain subsides.
And this method of cautery is called cautery of the cow.  (Note:  It's likely a misspelling or a regional variation; "vacas" means cows in Spanish, but the context suggests it's related to the goat horn.)
Chapter 42.
On the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes toothache is caused by various reasons, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and by similar causes, by the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that single type which is caused by an effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuating the patient with purging pills and similar remedies,  make incisions on the tooth where the pain is located, three lines according to its width, marking with ink five cauterizations per line or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and constitution.
And the cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or hunchback).
Often this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it happens to their structure in the hip with elevation and movement.
And you will find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And therefore when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in the whole part of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then care for the place until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Of the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is the custom of physicians to call this properly gout.
And so if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of cure and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is so that you cauterize that near evacuation in the area of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize it and do not press too hard with your hand with the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then apply the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called  (a type of) hand pain/rheumatism, then puncture in the circle of the knuckles as they curve, puncture in those two lines.
And if the pains remain in the fingers, then puncture in the circle of the knuckles on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise in a few days to the hips or muscles, then cauterize them in every part of the affected area.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and the reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, with the intestines protruding before the scrotum, and this is just beginning, then you should order the patient to cease eating that day and to use soothing remedies so that the tear in his abdomen may close.
Then hold the patient between your hands over his abdomen, and instruct him to hold his breath until the hernia or intestines are retracted.
Then return that [the hernia/intestines] with your fingers and gently push the rupture against the pubic bone, noting that the extremity of it should resemble half a circle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and let another servant be on their legs, and another on their feet, who should hold their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the hip joint in the middle of the thigh; and repeat with it another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe carefully the protrusion of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete/successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small, according to their size and age.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scar is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be reduced in work and in the repletion of food and drink, and in vehement voices, for when he uses this regimen of entire healing, he will be completely healed.
And I will say the treatment for ruptures in the other parts of the belly; and so, at the beginning, if you want them not to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size.  And let that which is exuded from the cautery be as much as a third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is suitable/appropriate.
And already some earlier authors have said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this shape, since it is marked with a probe/tent:
and a line is placed which goes across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion (or twisting).
When in some member, due to a fall or blow, pain arises and persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be for each member according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the twisting and pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be punctuated from one member to the next adjacent member, wherefore it is necessary that you treat these with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes treated with cautery with great benefit, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize that, consider whether the leprosy is spreading and whether it can be cured with what we have said in the division; and if it does not diminish, nor ceases, and you fear that the corruption will encompass the whole of his body and his entire complexion, then cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second lower, near the forehead at the hairline; and two more on the temples (two horns); and one at the back, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press your hand slightly inward so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the crusts are removed, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is alleviated from it; and cauterize over the spleen as is necessary.
And if the leprosy is already spread over the patient and manifestly appears, then you must cauterize him with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the coccyx, and one on the coccyx, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the buttocks, and one on each buttock, and with two on the knees, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the ankles of his feet; and cauterize him on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the knuckles of his hands.
And beware of the nerves which are on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and with another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient feels no pain from the cautery, as they do from wounds, because his body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of instruments.
And then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization for numbness/stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, near the origin of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is learned in the anatomy of the limbs and the origin of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is chronic and you do not apply any medicinal ointments to it, then you cauterize it with a cautery iron a little, according to the size and nature of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with barley and lentil flour with rose oil and arugula leaves, and with pigeon's blood or hen's blood—of each an equal part, and all mixed together—then apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system where L = 50 in Roman numerals)
Concerning the cauterization of swellings (or abscesses).
When a swelling is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or to the age of the patient, because they are old and decrepit, with little blood, or because of the time of year, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the swelling; then cauterize around its circumference with many small, punctate cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place that in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower down, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
