Concerning the cauterization of corrosives.
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that has suffered cauterization with fire, then you should ignite very small, and large (according to what is suitable for the place where) nails as cauteries in the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply crushed salt mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs and all the corruption are eradicated.
And then treat it with flesh-generating ointments.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that serves in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of quicker help.
And I have already described the treatment of it with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times there happens in the parts below the foot a disease that is called a wound, and it is a large, penetrating cause which injures the foot; and cautery in these cases is done in two ways, that is to say cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is that you heat the concave cautery resembling a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size which contains the wound in its entirety, and should be of fine edges.
And then place that heated iron on the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed eschar. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallow mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not healed, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should administer cautery with water in those cases.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand; and you rotate it with the compression of your hand completely, and let the edge of the cannula be pressed into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; and let the patient endure the sting of the hot water for a while, about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those [wounds] and then with the others until you have treated all of them that are in the body.
And then treat those [sores] after the roots of the wounds are eradicated with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
Concerning the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of cold origin, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cautery; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And his tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
Concerning the cauterization of accidental blemishes on the body.
When blemishes appear on the body, and there are pustules and fissures caused by thick, corrupted matter; then at the beginning, as it appears, it should be cauterized over the head of the fistula with a fine cautery, with a myrtle stick whose end is heated in a fire; or with a long root of erestologia; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is also suitable at the beginning of its expulsion, according to this manner; and they are not believed [to be effective], and the superfluity is broken up by doing this, and heals from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done near an evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a bleeding flux caused by an arrow wound.
Many times there is a flux from an arrow wound which is incised around the wound, which comes from outside, or around the incision of an abscess or cauterization of the limb, and similarly, and it is difficult to stop this.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand on the orifice of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it tightly many times until the flow of blood stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
And then put small and large olive cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of these, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, on that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until the bleeding is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the orifice of the artery when you remove your finger and remove the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from the cauteries which are prepared in the fire, and do not cease to do this one after another until the bleeding is restrained.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to restrain that blood, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities are better burned and the blood is restrained, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines be placed upon it whose virtue is to restrain blood; and they should be tightened with a cushion with a decent constriction.
But that which administers decent restriction with a ligature and with constriction with cloths or by the application of burning substances and the like, does not confer [its effect] except slowly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently open with the index finger over the orifice of that vein as we have said, and attend to the great constriction of it until the blood is stopped from the superior part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from over the artery; and over the artery let it be sprinkled with very cold water of great coldness until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider what need there is of a cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cauterization with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one seeking this will be relieved, then about that which he will have requested.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is that which is terrifying in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution, for in the operation which is in this chapter, many times there is an evacuation which is with blood, with which life is sustained, from the opening of a vein or incision on an abscess, or opening of a wound, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and their like. And with all of these are accompanied fear and trembling, and in the worst of them, death.
And I warn you that this hesitation should not fall upon you, for sometimes there falls upon you the handling of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them there is some who are angry with their illness and death is light upon him, and this because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his wealth and says, you hoping with that for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you of which this is the manner; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume any cause for this except through certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which follows praiseworthy success.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is returned, for in that is to you help in the acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and of glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you His understanding and not deny you guidance and protection, for that is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that that which you ask of this and of which you have need may be relieved for you.
Chapter One.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children during childbirth and when the midwife constricts the child's head without skill.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is not known.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is, in my experience, a fatal affliction for them.
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either collected between the skin and the bone, or it is collected under the bone on the brain.
Because if the humidity is between the skin and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two finger-widths so that the humidity can drain, and this is the shape of the spatula:
And so if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand from the inside, and this is not hidden from you. You should make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then tighten the fissures with cloths and lint; and then bathe it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and tighten the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the accumulation of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second/Chapter 2.
When someone suffers from cataracts or rheum in the eyes or in the feet, and this is chronic, and the cure is not obtained through medicines, then the final cure in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore it is necessary that, when you wish to incise them, the patient raises his head, and you should rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and the patient's neck should be constricted with some cloth; and you should consider where the vein pulsates; and the locations of each [artery] in the areas near the ears are compressed, and are thus hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with the sharp point of a scalpel with an incision extending to the bone, and let this be done along the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers, because when a vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the distance of three fingers, and then mark it with a probe, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let is about six ounces, and you should quickly let out more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any part of the splinter remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And then bind the wound with lint and linen bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrimonious humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or if it is an old and severe headache similar to these, and it is treated with medical methods of cure and this does not help, then we must try in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and look for the artery appearing in the temple because it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except in a few people by the force of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with warm water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then put on it ointment and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the [surgical] hook, and then incise a part of it according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and blood will not flow, for if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords of linen, and do not allow it putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-cautery until its ends are incised, and it will suffice for that same operation.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion; because cautery is among those things that help to dry out humors, and therefore it is better in this case.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you clean the place with old cotton, and that suitable powders are placed on it, and then it should be treated with dry medicines that promote the growth of flesh and with dressings until it is healed.
And if, while you are operating again, bleeding occurs from the artery, then you must attend to staunching that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with cotton, and pressing with your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those remedies are at hand, then place your finger on the area until the blood clots, and bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and apply each, very hot, until they reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. But however, if it has less sharpness than two knives it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives close to each other, it will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since in those [cauteries] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is cut lightly.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
Of the treatment of sharp and acrid eye inflammations.
When there are acrid tears, and it is the inflammation of those veins which are on the surface of the head, and you don't find any cause for treatment with medicine, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel in their forehead a crawling as if it were the crawling of ants, and their eyes are swollen, moist so that the hairs around them are matted, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancients used various methods in the treatment of this condition, and they called this treatment "spassa.
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures on the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the tear duct, and the third on the other side.
And be careful of the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and lengthen your hand from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers adjusted.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or dried cloths ready with which to dry the blood.
And then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/spatula] the whole area which is between the two incisions, with the scalpel which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then withdraw that spatula and introduce into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [curette] against the flesh attached to the bone, and the blunt edge towards the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the bone, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old linen, and put on these powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the treatment of tears due to cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences sharp and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and weakened, and their sight is impaired, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin shed, and there is sharp, throbbing pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the cataracts and matter originate from many veins proceeding from the inner parts.
And the best treatment in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand from the temples' lacerations, which are made sore by chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders boiled in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary to scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen which promotes the growth of flesh, thus: if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and from this make a plaster, and apply it to these wounds to encourage the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron or glass, or it is from a plant, such as a seed or a bean, or it is a fluid substance such as water and sour wine and things similar to them, or some animal.
And so when a stone falls into the ear or another similar cause of those things which do not soften or grow in the ear, and expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a suitable remedy, and close the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards at the top, for many times it is expelled with these actions.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a slender ear-picker, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the picker, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction with a slender probe of little doubling.
And so if it is not expelled by this, then make a small tube of brass and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open but the tube. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times this has been done as we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit flax or a wick prepared for catching birds, and place that on the end of a rod, and then carefully wrap a decent cotton around that. And then insert that into the opening of the ear easily, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And so if it is not expelled by all the means we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it out before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the patient's strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below the cartilage in the well-prepared place. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone; and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the thing causing the problem in the ear is a growth that is growing and swollen, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not yield to such treatment, then take finely powdered litharge according to this method: and use that for the excision of the growth that has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and apply it on small pieces of cloth, and then remove them with tweezers or a delicate instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing as a first resort, and fill their ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several heated stones and pebbles, according to the length of the finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and let the patient insert one of these into the opening of their ear and jump on one foot on that side and strike with another stone upon the stone that is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or a wick, and place one end of it in the ear and apply the other end to a fire until the majority of it is burned.
And then put in another wick and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula as described above for the extracting stone.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, such as a fly or similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is of large size, which appears to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and its cause is lighter than all the other causes that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you cure it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your cure does not succeed, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And then, if any cause of the worms appears there, extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula that is narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it with strong suction, and do this many times until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you plug and close the circumference of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the cure of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And then let it be such that it pushes what is in the cavity of the cannula continuously where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (possibly referring to a thin, flexible instrument) and wrap its extremity decently with cotton, and then drop oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula — and that is in the ear — and then introduce the ray with cotton on top and press with your hand that, with a moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be warmed a little with fire. And be careful that the substance which you instill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born without perforated ears, and this also happens to some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to injury or flesh growing there; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the entrance of the ear canal, which prevents hearing, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the entrance, which prevents hearing, is in most cases difficult to cure.
And in the case where hearing is possible, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and examine it. And then, if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, which has this form:
If its end is narrow, one side being sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then, if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove that with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the lobe of the ear, then take a fine, thin probe and heat it slightly with fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with what the patient easily finds, due to difficulty in hearing, gently force the obstruction; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then, place in the ear a piece of linen according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for some days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take a wick and soak it in rosewater and sprinkle it with finely crushed seeds and use it; and if you see the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the wick and replace it with another wick which you will wrap in a cerate made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely drained; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which develops on the eyelids.
Styes which develop on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with tweezers, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then place a poultice on the affected areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes a certain growth which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of its size it is called a swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling is proceeding in such a way that it appears from the eyelid and is spread throughout; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it lengthwise, and then peel it off entirely easily until it comes off, and then suspend it with a hook and excise it.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not suffer harm from it because the ointment heals and closes it.
And if the growth is leaning toward the inner parts of the eyelid, beyond the inner surface, then raise the eyelid and suspend the growth with a hook,  without making an incision, and press it from all sides; and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, that harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a growth on the upper eyelid.
A growth (or excrescence) is a grey mass which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and especially affects children, it obscures their eyes and causes them to have watery eyes. And they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears well up in them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are moist, which already have a swollen covering, and the imminence of a growth is apparent in the eye; and that when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the growth within.
And a certain manner of operation is this: the patient rests their head on your lap, and you place a cloth between you and them, and make a circle of it according to the size containing the whole growth, and place that over it and press with your fingers all around so that the growth is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then make an incision in the middle of that swelling with a sharp lancet, the incision being no larger than that made in bloodletting.
But in the incision it is necessary that you cut completely through until you reach the growth, for in most cases the growth yields to the incision as a white fatty substance.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is completely removed.
And be careful not to go too deep in the incision so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the growth does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then continue the incision a little gently until the growth appears; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar, and place them on the area and compress it with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it absorbs the remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with emollient plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Of the ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, hairs grow beneath the natural hairs and are ingrown, then these harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this becomes a cause of destruction of the eye, which is called a  "tactinir" of the eye. It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine as previously stated in the chapter on cautery; or the tactinir is treated by incision and suture; or by tubes, as I shall explain.
Therefore, it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into that which is closest to the part below the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate upwards, and this should be — near the edge of the eyelid from which the hairs grow — near the hairs, and pull the hair upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with the ray [of light].
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid below the ingrown hairs with a sharp instrument from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct; and then extract the hair and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bind it over the eyelid with a bandage, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies among men, for some it is necessary that a large amount be incised from the eyelid according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
Then make an incision with the instrument along the two lines which you have marked, beginning from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, let it be like the thickness of a king's [hair].  (The meaning is slightly ambiguous here; "rey" likely refers to a royal's hair, implying a very fine cut.)
Then place the ointment in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and apply what is superfluous of the threads to the two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its healing; but suture is better, and this is what the ancients said, to be precise.
However, let there be diligence on the part of the surgeon, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in him/her.
And there is another way to treat it, which is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three ointments, separated and combined according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the ointments, and this is not equal, then take a needle on which there is thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and the second thread near the smaller lacrimal duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with those, raise your hand with equal elevation, raise the whole eyelid on which it is marked, as we have recounted.
And then join the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and something similar, and with ointment.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then you should use what we have said about incision; however, suture in each case is more skillful.
To bind with splints is according to this form: and it is that you should turn back the eyelid and incise the figure that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two splints or two thin rods, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and whose width should be less than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each [splint] with subtlety over what is superfluous of the eyelid's skin, and bind the splints to each part with a strong binding, and leave it for some days, because the growth, due to the binding, will die and dry up and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then cure the wound until it is healed.
Then, once it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two splints which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle, and place on it a fine silken thread and lion [possibly referring to lion's hair or a type of fine thread], and carefully adjust its ends so that you do not injure the eye in any way. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm's width.
And then you prepare another short, fine thread below it, and tie its ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which needs to be removed, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out and insert it into a container, and let the hairs be one or two or three and no more.
And then pull your hand with it until the hair is extracted with tweezers from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled with tweezers and the hair is not removed, you will pull the tweezers downwards with the thread which you have composed with it, until the tweezers are removed from the wound above.
And examine it with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the tweezers and the thread, and then clean and close the wound, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened by some space, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a stye appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos, and this stye is either natural or accidental, and it is caused by the consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, or similar poorly healed conditions.
The method of operation is that you should incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not advisable that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do that, the stye will return worse than it ever was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice with the water in which mallows and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oil, and soak the linen cloth in it, and treat it with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you should strive with all your skill that it does not consolidate in a different way than it was at the beginning.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing but a correction of the stye so that it does not return to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which is formed on the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms on the lower eyelid—which is truly called a stye—is of two kinds: one natural, the other accidental.
But the accidental one is caused by a wound, or a cut, or cautery, or other similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And if the eyelid then returns to its proper shape, it is good; but if not, take a splint and place it in the split, and with it reshape the eyelid, and sew the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be at two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be made of them so that when they are brought together, a figure is made of them.
And this figure is the Greek letter: Lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh on that side, the amount that is the width of the sharp lower eyelid.
And then join the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if an abscess develops through a fissure, suture, or cautery, then you should make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid as previously described. And then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips, as we have said.
In summary, concerning the treatment of an abscess whether above or below, the operation should proceed as prepared for the form of the abscess, which presents in many different forms.
And the skillful surgeon always prepares the instruments in every way possible so that he may restore the form to its natural state, or close to it, and the patient, according to what suits him in terms of the operation and the instruments, in the best conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in such a way that the eyelid is solidified above with the white of the eye, and its natural movement and function are impeded, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid and raise it, or spread it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed for the incisions, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision gently separate that adhesion until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the adhesion is completely healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it honey dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small, blunt instrument, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and is similar to hard, fine horn; or it is non-nervous, similar to congealed white moisture, which when an iron touches it, or is forced to take hold with an instrument, is cut and the instrument is not embedded in it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct until it grows little by little to cover the sight and prohibits light and the movement of the eye.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head on your lap, and then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with a small, double-ended instrument and extend it towards the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and place it in the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula or with small scissors, and leave the flesh of the natural tear duct so that through the incision of that, a continuous flow of tears does not occur.
And sometimes the nail, with only the instrument extended, or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, after the incision, put a little crushed salt or a powder made of cyclamen into the eye, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it fixed to the nail bed with the instrument, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed in the manner I have recounted in the division [or section].
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the patient's eye and take a thin lion spatula of sharp steel, according to this form: with subtlety.  (Or: delicately; with precision) And if you see that it is rarefied and is congested and rooted there, then instill into the eye from your syringe a powder made of cyclamen, or with rue, or with crushed salt, and occlude the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all that is in it is removed, unless, on the contrary, a hot abscess forms in the eye; for then you leave it alone, and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed. (Or: ...until it is cured; ...until it is made whole)
The prominence of the lacrimal flesh, if it harms the eye greatly, should be suspended with a hook; and incise it on one side, and do not extend the incision lest you cut the flow of tears.
Then instill into the lacrimal duct rue and cyclamen powder until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a stye and that which is born of flesh adhering to the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is grown, and it is raised until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids and strongly closes the eyelids at the outer parts and it is similar to a rose of a balustrade (or, a rose-shaped growth).
But the operation is that the patient places his head on your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and collect all that flesh with instruments convenient for that, and hold that with forceps or with scissors; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and guard the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision, with which you incise those things, be with a spatula which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which hair is collected, according to how the form of it will come to this chapter.
Then when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place over the eye externally a cotton compress with egg white until it is secured from hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is seen, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with that which will yield that abscess, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do with the fleshy growth which happens in the eye of this kind.
But however, you should avoid all treatment of the fragile eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member can tolerate that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is a collection of red veins which are hardened on the eye and impede vision by their action, and they weaken the eye over a long period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and has no other disease than pus; then you collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an oncia or two, according to your judgment; and let that oncia be of subtle doubling according to this form:
Or two oncias gradually in one piece according to this form:
And then those veins should be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins being removed from the eye and are retracted.
And beware that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is at midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation, that you only incise those veins.
And when you have completed it, then you distil into the eye verdigris or rue so that with its sharpness it corrodes what has been removed from the eyelid.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster it all with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is soothed and the risk of a hot abscess is avoided.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of a deviated nose.
When you treat a deviated nose with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as narrated previously, and it is not healed, then there is no other remedy than to make incisions on the abscess before its maturation, and to extract all the humidity which is within it and the pus, until the bone is uncovered.
And so, when the bone is uncovered and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round, like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle sharpening (as is shown in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the place with drying and astringent substances.
Then, if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown there and the body of the wound is closed and remains so for forty days, and it is not renewed and is not festering and no accident happens to it, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other way but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this method:  that is, that you uncover the underlying bone with an iron instrument or a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine of this shape:
Let the extremity of the iron trephine be triangular, and its shaft be slender and tapering towards the extremity, like this.
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal duct; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates all the bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel air escaping when you move your hand.
Then restore the area with styptic and drying medicines as you have been taught.
Then, when the flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
On the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes entirely and is not injured by a wound, diminution, or weakening, then you should reassure the patient and watch over them. Then apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction for good evacuation; and then apply to the eye an ointment which is made from acanthus, aloe, incense and sarcocolla, and then firmly attach the plaster to the eye with many powders.
And the method of the powders is that you take many lion cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make many of these.
And then place those one upon the other according to the amount that the eye requires. And then bind them tightly with a hard cloth with a firm bandage; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is weakened, then it should be bound very tightly.
And then remove the bandage during the day and night, as we have said.
And if sometimes the eye is already reduced, it is good, and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the bandage and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the prominence of the uvea. (Uvea here refers to the uvea of the eye)
When a rupture occurs in the uvea, and it protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then place another needle with double thread from the lacrimal side, and make that penetrate and release the first needle according to its position; and then incise the area of the double thread and lift with that the part of the uvea in the circumference of the needle, and tighten that tightly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and allow the thread to adhere to the uvea.
And then treat the eye with something that will comfort it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth around the eye a large prominent area, and then it should be perforated with a fine spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and the albuminous humor will run, and the eye will descend and enter immediately. (This refers to a procedure likely involving draining fluid from the eye).
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of alconati.  (This likely refers to a specific ailment or condition, the exact nature of which would require further context from the treatise.)
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a swelling which is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that. (The description suggests a watery swelling or discharge in the eye, different from a simple watery discharge.)
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, and then take their head in your hands on both sides and manipulate it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so it doesn’t descend, and the light should be obstructed, and the patient should see things as they were seen. (This describes a procedure for treating the eye swelling, involving positioning the patient, manipulating the head to encourage drainage, and controlling light exposure.)
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water. (This indicates that if the swelling doesn't drain, it's diagnosed as being purely watery in nature.)
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and use it on the eyelid, carefully around the juncture of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus (towards the empty space), until the swelling is expelled. (This describes an alternative procedure using a spatula to manually remove the swelling if the initial method fails.  The "empty space" likely refers to the area where the swelling is visible.)
Then, once it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey. (This describes post-procedure treatment involving instilling a honey and/or fenugreek solution into the eye.)
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed. (This indicates further treatment is needed to complete the healing process.)
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of watery eye discharge in the division and which ones should be perforated, declaring this according to the health condition. (This refers back to a previous section describing different types of eye discharge and appropriate treatment based on those types.)
Then you should have the patient sit between your legs on a chair and bind their healthy eye, tying it tightly.  (This describes positioning the patient and temporarily occluding the unaffected eye.)
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye with the watery discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one. (This explains which hand to use depending on which eye needs treatment.)
And then with your right hand, take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one. (This instructs the physician to use the opposite hand to hold an almond, presumably to apply it as part of the treatment.)
And then place the end of the almond near the cornea, along the width of the iris in the white of the eye, on the side of the smaller lachrymal duct, and then gently press the almond and rotate your hand with it. (This describes the precise application of the almond to the eye.)
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space/cavity. (This indicates the almond should be pushed into a specific area of the eye.)
And it should be that the path of the almond to the preon (presumably a landmark) is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness (iris), and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with this pupil through the clarity of the corneal tunic.  (This is a complex sentence describing the proper distance to apply the almond. "Preon" is likely a medical term not easily translatable without further context.)
And then place the almond into the place where the water (presumably aqueous humor) is, and then you draw it down one time and then another, and if the water descends immediately, then you see the patient whose sight is immediately restored, and the almond in their eye. (This describes the manipulation of the almond to drain fluid, leading to improved vision.)
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you repeat that again before the almond is removed. (This advises a period of rest and potential repetition of the procedure.)
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond easily, and gently rub with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in water and with that wash the eye inside; and then place outside the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this. And if the almond does not obey you upon entry into the eye due to its hardness — for there are some men whose eyes are very hard — then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is named alberid, of which this is the form: (This explains an alternative approach if the almond is difficult to insert due to the hardness of the eye.  It introduces a specific instrument, the "alberid," for the procedure.)
And then perforate that joint with it, and do not thrust your hand into the hole, and it is only that you make a subtle path for the almond. (This describes the use of the alberid to create a small opening, advising against excessive force.)
And then introduce the almond as we have said. (This is a simple instruction to proceed with the almond insertion, referring back to earlier instructions.)
And for the completion of your work, let a sleeping place be prepared for the patient, decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room, and let all movements be prohibited. (This outlines post-procedure care: rest in a dark, quiet place to promote healing and prevent disruption.)
And place him in such a position that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.  (This further emphasizes the need for stillness and proper positioning during recovery, ensuring the patient remains aligned and doesn't move their head.)
And let the bandage remain according to its disposition until the third day. (This instructs to leave the dressing undisturbed until the third day.)
And then unbind it in that dark room and avoid exposing it to strong light; show him some things, and then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. (This describes a gradual process of loosening and removing the bandage,  limiting light exposure, and then re-bandaging at a later date.)
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the cures or immediately after the infusion. (This indicates that the procedure described should not be performed during or immediately after other treatments or medication.) Furthermore, it is necessary that you avoid this because the water may easily rise due to great and vehement observation/scrutiny. (This suggests that close observation or examination of the affected area might cause a problematic reaction, possibly the water rising, so the procedure should be handled carefully and with minimal observation.)
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the wound before the seventh day and rectify it with something that cures the abscess. (This provides an exception to the previous instructions: if an abscess develops, the bandage should be removed earlier and the abscess treated.)
Then you blindfold him and place a cloth or mask over his face, by which his sight is regulated, leaving him in a dark room for some days. (This describes a treatment involving blindfolding the patient and keeping them in a dark room for a period of time.)
And then he should be moved from that place gradually and should think about the causes of his ailment.  (This suggests a gradual transition out of the dark room and a focus on the potential causes of the illness, perhaps as part of a broader healing process.)
And know that in surgery, the student is not excused from seeing some things, and then you should be bold/confident in the operation. (This indicates that even students should observe certain procedures and that the practitioner should have confidence in their surgical skills.)
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and someone said that they made a fire in Ayrac, a perforated vessel, with which the water is sucked up. Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of rotten flesh in the nose.
When various fleshy growths appear in the nose, some of which resemble a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, ulcerated, of dark color; and from it is what is lion flesh, not of dark color. (This describes different types of flesh growth: cancerous, ulcerated and dark, and another, "lion flesh", which is not dark).
Therefore, in the case of these flesh growths that are not malignant or cancerous ("lion flesh"), you should make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, open their nostrils, and gently touch those growths with your finger. Then extract that [growth] from the outer parts, and then cut away what you have grasped of it with a sharp, thin spatula, from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed;  if any part remains from which excision is possible, then you shall scrape that with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or is a hot, inflamed abscess, then apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you should incise and cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all cavities are removed.
Then let there be poured into the nose, near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and no moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the deeper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Then it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into it, to which some thickness has been added, and tie knots on it, placing between each knot the amount of a finger or less, and instruct the patient to introduce one end of the thread into the nose with a rod or with any other instrument that is possible for them.
And after this has been done, let them draw in its scent until it reaches the nasal passages and is expelled through their throat, for often children at school do something similar, and it is an easy thing for whoever wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end protruding from the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then apply sawdust mixed with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now together with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread, and immediately after the extraction of the blood, apply a poultice to the nose, and do this for three days or more until the ointment has corroded all that is rotten flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need more healing than this requires, use that (other method/remedy).
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small shell, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open; to which there should be a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat in it the oil or water that you want, from the juices and from the suctible substances.
Chapter 25.
Of nasal polyps in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a polyp grows in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in some days, to the point that it greatly obstructs the patient's breathing.
And for that reason it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate the whole thing. And then place on the location either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, hard, of a dark color and of little feeling; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; because many times to him who incises this abscess happens great harm from that which it had.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and is not of a dark color, and sometimes incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with that which dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should place little confidence in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and afterwards treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scrape each part of its apparent surface until blood comes out. Then join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed [ingredients], and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the threads are shed by themselves, and then treat it with ointment until it heals.
But the manner of suturing is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which come into the two edges in the inner parts.
Many times there appear in the inner parts of the labia of men certain hard abscesses similar to the kernels of cherries, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the labia and incise each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then fill the place of the incision with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then it should be washed with sharp vinegar and the place treated with something styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Very often there grows on the gum a fleshy growth which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that up with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
And then place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for then the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, there are rough, sticky crusts, and sometimes they are blackened and become yellow or green, so that the gum is affected by this corruption and the teeth become loose.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and place his head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar substances appear, until nothing remains of them, and this removes the blackness, yellowness and other things until they are cleaned.
Then if this which is in the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth on the next day and the second and the third until you arrive at the final result that you desire.
And know that the teeth require many instruments of diverse forms and shapes according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the instrument with which the tooth is scraped on the inner part is different from the instrument with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms, and these forms of instruments should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that supplants its sight when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance in which there is an excuse for its extraction.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is aching, for many times the pain deceives the patient and they believe it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is aching, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps, beginning little by little until it is completely loosened.
And then apply strong, large forceps for a good grip or firm hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold them well and they do not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not removed, then take one of those instruments, and place it under it on all sides with ease, and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or decayed, then you should fill that hole with cloth and bind it tightly with a thin, fine thread so that it is not broken during the time you are grasping it with forceps.
And you must carefully open [the gum] with scarification around all parts of the gum, and take great care that you do not break it and leave a part of it behind, and it will return to the patient with an affliction greater than their initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity in extraction, without administering what we have recounted; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain, all or some of them, or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.
And then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding results from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of sage and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this sage does not work, cauterize [it].
The shape of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extremity, short and thick handle, so that they are not bent when you have to work with them.
And let them be like tongs with a thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent; let the extremities be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron, skillfully made with straight ends, and at the ends of those let there be teeth, of which the ones interlock with the others and tighten by strong constriction; and sometimes the ends of these have the form of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the extraction of teeth and the removal of broken jawbones.
When you must extract a tooth which is broken and some cause remains, then you must place a cotton compress soaked in a potion on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then insert tongs or pincers, the ends of which resemble the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Let their ends be made inside like a file or rasp.

And let it be of short, somewhat thick extremity, and let it not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is removed, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.

of the triangular, curved or short extremity, which should have some thickness, and should not be bent so that it is not broken.

But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is possible, then let it be cured in its place with what is convenient for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have described in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a splint, of which this is the form:
