But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, distorting their shape, especially in women and particularly older women; then you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and neither extraction nor filing is possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you incise it with an instrument of which this is the form; and it is similar to a boar's tusk, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done over several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other tooth is injured.
And if the protruding tooth is suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the form:
And let the entire file be of Indian iron and very smooth; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some scrapers.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary that you smooth it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and leveled, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated this with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, contrive that they are fastened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and almost powders after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of binding is that you take the wire and pass the double of it between two healthy teeth, and then let it grow with two twists of the wire between two damaged teeth, or if one or several, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends in each place with a file, and bend them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus sometimes these are bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to their proper places, and are grown back as we have recounted, and that they remain fixed.
And this is only done by good and skilled artisans.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is used, and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is fastened as we have said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and lift their tongue, and carefully incise that nervous ligament to the extent that the tongue is freed from its restriction.
But if in that area there is some hardness or knot, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should apply an unguent and incise that area to the extent that you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
And carefully wash the area of the incision with warm water or with wine vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is to say, a cloth which the patient keeps under the tongue every night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess similar to a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it quickly becomes enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black in color, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancerous.
And if it is tending towards white, and is moist, then you place an oncia (ounce, possibly referring to a surgical instrument) there, and incise it with a fine scalpel, and incise it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then you apply crushed vitriol to it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sharp wine and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Concerning the extraction of an abscess from both ears, and of that which is located in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When a swelling occurs in the throat, these are glands which are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called the tonsils; when you treat them with what we have said in the division [previous section of the text] and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive; then do not touch it with an iron instrument.
And if it is red and the root is large, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then you will either extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that ought to be incised.
And the operation for it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already been calmed by complete rest, or if it has diminished in any way; and then have the patient sit before the sun, and their head should be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the assistant, taking it between their hands, should press down the tongue with a very fine instrument, of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or bronze, and let it be thin like a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with that [instrument] and the abscess is shown to you, and you have looked at it carefully, then take an oncia [ounce] and insert it into the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can. Also, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Also, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each of them is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner of incision, and after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, let him gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and then cure it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those near the two ears, and incise those as I have said in the incision of two sides equally.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was tending towards a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman breathed with a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her to eat and drink water, and she was near death if she had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had made two openings through which it exited.
Then I treated it with a fistula, and I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out, and then I incised it from where I understood it came from the other opening, and then I opened its mouth, and I compressed its tongue, and then I placed the probe into that abscess.
And then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the woman's throat was completely cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, and then she took food, so I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess succeeded it, which I incised until it was eliminated.
Then I cauterized her throat, then I stopped the bleeding; and then I walked around and I didn't know what God would do about it then.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular [abscess]; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a buba (or swelling).
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and your treatment is not effective, and you see that the cauterized abscess has already been removed and it is subtle, then you should incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you should delay the incision, because in that case there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, its whiteness and length,  make the patient sit and press down their tongue with an instrument which follows its shape, and then place the lancet on the uvula and draw it down and incise it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the incision of the tonsils.
And you should not incise it except what is within the natural limits without addition, for if you incise more, you will impair their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use caution in cauterizing it without causing fear, and the method of cauterizing it is only with a sharp instrument; and you should place the patient's head in your lap, and then press down their tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a live coal, and do not apply it too thickly nor thinly, and with it fill the concavity of the instrument which has this form:
Let the end of it be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that the saliva or mucus from their mouth flows out because of the intrinsic medicine, so that nothing of it descends into their throat and harms them.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it against the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried up by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton in a cannula from above so that the cotton adheres to that cannula, and do this as many times as it takes to achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it so that it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it, and after the cauterization, clean the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and let it be washed with cold water, and let it be treated externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, dried Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged in sour wine, and boil; and let the pot be well sealed with suitable lute, and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot's lid, over which is placed a concave brass instrument in this manner; it should be made of silver or brass.
And let the end [of the instrument] be inserted into the patient's mouth so that the vapor may reach the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, for it would increase the abscess too much, and you should not do that, but rather you should cauterize the abscess at its beginning.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a cane and attach a broken eggshell to its end, so that the patient's mouth is covered, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
On the extraction of thorns and that which adheres to the throat and other causes.
Many times, a fish bone or some other thing adheres to the throat.
Then it is necessary that you extract what is apparent of those things, on which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that that which is in the throat may be shown to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of dried sea sponge, and bind it tightly with thread, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, it will extract them, and then they will be expelled. And also, take an eel and bind it tightly with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly remove it.
But if they are not expelled by those methods which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be somewhat thicker than a finger, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you may easily introduce into his throat.
And let him have his head raised up, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, lest it cause him pain; and with that, push the bone or thorn, or let the physician do it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself insert it, because of his knowledge of the location of the lodged cause, and push it down, and pull his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the amount prepared for him, until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not work, then consider [applying] to the patient's throat a leech, provided that you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with delicate, appropriate gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this several times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat), and abstain the patient from water all day.
And then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it and agitate the water every now and then with your hand, because the leech, as soon as it feels the water, falls off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cymene or with asafoetida, using the instrument which we have recommended in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of this is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals, and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then let the fumigation come from above; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and restrain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and not drinking water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps, as sometimes, except that it has some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is curved, in which there is a roughness from filing, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many kinds, according to what is recalled of them, of each in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses in themselves; and because of what we understand of the humidities.
And the second way is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy location is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, there are some abscesses which should not be perforated except near the maturation of the pus within them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw, and are not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until it can consume what is in its circumference, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that limb is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, therefore perforate that which is not matured; and it should not be perforated raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the buttocks, so that its pus may not spread and be elongated to the inner parts of the buttocks, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it is not healed. But you must know the time for the perforation of the abscess, and that is when it is matured.
And you will know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and cessation of the fever, and also a diminution of the redness and pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know that the perforation falls in the place lowest in the abscess and from which the cure of the swelling comes.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and, finally, in all even places where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you delay its perforation until its digestion is completed according to what we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the boil be hollowed out and incised, when it is made like bread and is ripe, as happens in cases of buboes and boils.
And some of those are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar ones.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, it is necessary that you perforate it with a superficial, or only planar, perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, it is necessary that you perforate it and do not try to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted a part of it, then bandage the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and do this many times according to gradation until it is completely evacuated.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the egress of pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you must operate on them carefully.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning the perforation with which you perforate these abscesses, you must cleanse the wounds and consider then if the wound of the abscess is small, or if it is a planar incision, then you apply lint or old linen to it; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then it is necessary that you place a wick in each opening until they meet.
And if in the abscess a part of the flesh is excised or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then it is necessary that you fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted splinters or roots, without humidity; and bind it until the third day; and then proceed and treat it with the necessary ointments until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you moisten a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen and treatment and powders which I have described in many places in this our book and in other sections.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then it is necessary that you mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply those to the area.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then it is necessary that you apply powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then it is necessary that you open the abscess and clean it and administer to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the method of distinction I have already described each abscess and its treatment method in specific chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which appear in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses, and they are a kind of nodes which contain membranes that are attached to their surfaces; and their forms are varied.
And some of them are purulent, and those contain a humidity resembling pus. And some of them contain a humidity resembling dust, and similar to them; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as the extraction of these does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you explore them with an instrument whose shape is like this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G. Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you should scrape that skin which contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, because the expelled matter will return if any of it remains.
Then submerge a pledget in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode all that remains of that membrane.
And then repeat applying the pledget with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if needed, until you are confident that there is no remaining part of that membrane; then heal the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you should incise it according to this form: with an incision having the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from all parts, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if the arrowhead opposes you, then do what I have told you first.
And similarly proceed in the case of a stony abscess in incision and treatment as we have told you; and incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses, which are in the head in some men, have absolutely no moisture; the reason for which is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head besides this one, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses which come to the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them so that the body may be relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is suitable for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrophulas which happen in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they happen in other parts of the body.
And those that happen in the neck, and many that are generated among them, and every scrofula of these is within its own tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrophulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain humidity, and some of them are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition to the touch, of which the color that is manifest is close to the color of the flesh, and is softened in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, you should incise that with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, from line G to line B, and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most cautious that the vein or nerve is not incised.
And let the scalpel not be too sharp, lest you injure your hand with the incision, or that you disturb the patient, and that you incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery and prohibit you from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess rests and the wound is softened and begins to be purulent. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then investigate with your index finger if there is any cause of the other small scrophulas, then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you do not incise that scrofula at its root. Afterwards, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then care for it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then it is necessary that you adjust the two lips of the wound and stitch it immediately so that no superfluity remains.
But if you see that superfluity remains because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you understand how to incise its root, and extract it, and administer suture and what has been said.
And as for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and position the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the head of the abscess accidentally within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the abscess, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the description/writing of the speech/discourse of those (ancients).
But in men having a hernia, the extraction of the abscess head must be avoided, because no benefit is done by it, since all the veins and major (blood) vessels of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or in both ears, when the illness is not in the (respiratory) canal, it is necessary to use the incision of the abscess head to avoid the loss which is caused by perforation (leaving it untreated).
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the abscess head below the three circles of the abscess's circles, or four with a small incision in width in the area between two circles as regards quantity, so that the incision is in the soft tissue, not in the cartilage; and this location is suitable for incision because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is timid, then it is necessary that he extend the throat's membrane with a hook, and then split the membrane until reaching the canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them there.
And then incise the soft tissue as we have recounted; and the incision of the canal is indicated by the inflammation which has come from it with what is broken in it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, when the hour is reached in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's membrane, and sew that alone without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that produce flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the discourse which we have recounted, is an exposition not only that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come to death, and his breath is cut off; and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe over the site of the wound for some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for this reason I order that the wound be left open until the malignancy of the disease is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this I have seen with my own eyes, that a servant took a knife and put it to her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her treatment, and I found the groan as it is groaned by the dying, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had issued from the small wound.
Then I was convinced that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind was escaping from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And there happened to the servant nothing but hoarseness in her voice, and after a few days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the section of the tonsil there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Of incision upon an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and is very common in women.
And it is of two types, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural one is not possible to cure.
And the accidental one is of two types, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other type resembles an abscess which is formed by the knotting of an artery, and in this one there is danger. And one should not act presumptuously with iron (surgery) except in the case of a small one (abscess). And you should probe and investigate it with your finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you should incise it like the nodes, and extract it with that which contains it from the skin and bursa; and if not, then its entire being should be examined, and then treat the area with what is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision of types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division (of chapters).
And it is necessary that I recount here the difference between a node and an abscess, as there might be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and intense pain until the boiling of the superfluity is settled; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that leonine [lion-like]  cyphax which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is formed like a vesicle, be it larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (of chapters).
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, you must remove it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will turn your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the pus.
Then place your hand around it according to the size of the abscess, and then extract the intromes and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the running humidity, whatever its color, is expelled, then you should incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if you find no humidity in the wake of the intromes, then you know it is fatty.
And dry it out with an incision in the shape of a cross, according to this form: as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and easily scrape away the pus from all sides, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that matter with the node (or knot/swelling).
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the matter, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the swelling will return in the same manner.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place a warm poultice over it that will suppurate, and treat it with another treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large swelling, then you sew the two lips of it together and treat it with that which causes it to form flesh (i.e., promotes granulation and healing).
But if a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is nearby, then you consider it and fill the area with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise what remains of the swelling.

Chapter 47.
On the treatment of the breasts of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to excessive sleeping, as they begin to become hairy, and then the breasts are inflamed, and resemble the breasts of women, and remain as festering, whitish swellings.
And for someone who has this, it is necessary that you incise those with a lunate incision, according to this shape, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medication, and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and is softened by its size, as happens in women, then let it be incised in two of its upper parts, with two incisions similar to a lunar shape, each of which should be contained within the other at the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller one according to this shape of line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and the powders which are necessary in the treatment until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's discomfort and bleeding, then you should fill the wound with lint soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the perforation of abscesses that come under the nipple.
These abscesses that appear under the nipple are a type of hard scrofula, which contains a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old lint and leave it there until the next day.
Then the lint should be removed and treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula similarly.
But if the wound is extensive so that putrefaction occurs, then apply cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from archery or from a vein.
When an artery is broken, and the surrounding flesh is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein are these: because when the abscess is from an artery it is long and deeply situated in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel in it a sort of grating/creaking sound.
And that which is from a vein is round and superficial on the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and if it is very large, then its treatment with iron should be avoided; and that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should be avoided [i.e., incision should be avoided].
But concerning that which is there because of inflammation of the opening of the artery, make a longitudinal incision in the flesh; and then open the incision with forceps and approach the artery, and free it from the surrounding tissues until it is uncovered.
Then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places — a procedure which I have shown you in the extraction of two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood that is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
Then administer treatment where pus is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much of the abscess as is possible with the flesh, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature as I have told you in the ligature of a grape in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that you will pull out the thread, then insert the thread with that needle below the entire abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the flesh, and leave what is tied there.
Then place on it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with warm applications and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens because of a twisted nerve.
Just as an abscess happens in an artery and in a vein, similarly an abscess happens in the nerves when it happens by percussion or happens by excessive work, and similarly.
And it is made mostly in the locations of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are injured.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body parts, and it is in the largest of its dispositions without them, and when it is constricted with vehemence, the patient feels a kind of numbness there.
And the abscess is not aggregated in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the collarbone, and it is injured in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, because contraction will quickly occur.
But that which is in the head or on the forehead, incise it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and cut it away from the root.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system)
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the area that occurs in the abdomen.  (A more natural English rendering might be: "Concerning the incision of an abdominal abscess.")
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, those things which are called fungi, by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and indeed, the lips of these are inverted, and some are small, and some are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two such things which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with thin roots.
And indeed the lips of these were inverted and split open, and a moisture flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one about eighteen ounces, and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the growth is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let there be cauteries in the fire in your presence, for many times from the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind it to the growth, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead strongly around the growth, until it is severed and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the growth has a thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a growth which is of a dark color, with little feeling, and little bleeding, for that is undoubtedly cancerous.
And I come with the reminder of cancer concerning this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of a protrusion of the umbilicus.
A protrusion of the umbilicus is caused by many things; either by a fissure of the membrane which is over the belly, and from it the intestine or bowel, as happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or by wind constricted there.
But if it is due to a fissure of the membrane and a protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the exit of the bowel, then its location is as we have recounted, with more vehement variety, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and soon returns; and it is quickly in its place, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, inclining to blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is fixed in a certain amount.
But if it is due to wind/gas, then touching it is soft.
And the operation in this case is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to arterial blood, or venous blood, or due to wind/gas; then you must be prohibited from its treatment, because in that there is fear and danger, as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings from arteries and veins.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane, then you must order the patient to hold their breath and lie down; and then mark with a probe around the entire umbilicus, and then order them to lie between your hands on their back, and then surround with a wide spatula around the umbilicus over the area which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling upwards with a large inch; and then bind it around with strong thread or with a bandage, in a proper manner of binding.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and seek the intestine; and if you have found it and held it by the ligature, then you cautiously extract and push the intestine back into the interior of the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with tweezers; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is contrary to your [knowledge of] artery or vein, then circumcise that area, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put on those two strong threads and insert the two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you should make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then treat it with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is of flesh growing there or due to humidity, then it is necessary that you concavate the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find there, and then treat it with what is necessary and which will consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and that it is advisable to avoid treatment with iron so that it does not become ulcerated; and we have spoken of cancer and the great care in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract it all, and especially when it is small in the beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not meddle with it, for I have never been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone else who has succeeded in doing so.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and purge the patient and draw out some black bile, and then bleed him, if a clear plethora is found.
Then prepare the patient for the operation where it is located, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape away from all sides with a sharp spoon until no trace remains of its roots. And let the blood flow, and do not stop it lightly, and press the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instrument is available to you.
But if you find the flow of thick blood contrary [to your wishes] from an incision of a vein or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood stops, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy of water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, for that is mortal.
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is ineffective, then consider: for if the patient has already acquired debility, or has another illness along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain, and similar things, then be careful not to treat them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness except dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on them is that you raise the patient between your hands, and the attendant gently presses their abdomen with their hand, driving the water towards the area below the pubic bone, and then take a pointed lancet in this manner:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; but nevertheless let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in the operation with it you do not penetrate into the intestine and injure it.
And then consider if the generation of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, distance yourself from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, to the right of it above the pubic bone.
But if the generation of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then your incision should be on the right side.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the diaphragm, and you should scrape that; and if it is the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the diaphragm until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or latten, which at its lower part has a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its end is incised in the manner of a feather, like this:
And at its uppermost end is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately descends upon the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, for if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient will quickly die from the dissolution of their animal spirit, or they will suffer syncope which is near to death.
But evacuate according to the strength of the patient and according to what the patient's condition indicates to you by the strength of their pulse and their general well-being.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the chain which covers the hole that is on the cup, which I have described to you how to pierce in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for a few days cautiously, and guard against error so that only a little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient and leave a large quantity of water, then treat them with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose them to the sun for a long time; and treat them with drying medicines until they are healed.
And cauterize the stomach, spleen, and liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the treatment of children born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the opening of its urethra is not perforated (or is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore it is necessary to treat it with perforation immediately after birth, using a very fine instrument, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated, and it will urinate; and then replace the pin.
And if you do not place the lead pin, it is possible that the urine which goes to that place will not allow it to close.
But that in which the opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its place, so it is said, the cause of which is that in those who are born and the opening is below the end of the penis, so that they cannot urinate normally until they lift the penis with their hand upwards; and this is not a generative defect, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line, and it is a very prolonged illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the foreskin with a sharp instrument, like a feather incision, or that you shave a part, so that the middle of it comes to resemble the head of the penis and that the opening falls in the middle as needed.
And beware of bleeding during your operation, for that often happens; and stop the bleeding with something that will staunch it, and care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning phimosis, which occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis.
This phimosis often occurs on the penis and is an excessive growth of fleshy tissue.
And of that, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
For the non-malignant type, you should suspend it with a fine thread and excise it until it is entirely removed; then place on that wound a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment; and afterwards, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the phimosis is malignant, of a dark color, then you should apply cautery after incising it and its root.
But if the phimosis is on the prepuce of the glans penis that is uncircumcised, and one part of the phimosis is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the part that is inside first until it is healed; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat both at the same time, there is a risk of damaging the prepuce.
And if you find in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should excise everything that is already blackened and beginning to corrupt or already corrupted; and then apply to that area a poultice of honey, mixed with ground pomegranate rind and crushed and sifted herbs.
And then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
Because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for the flow of blood and for the wound when it is corrupted (infected).
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion only occurs in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess), you must scrape with a blunt spatula until the bond is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate them delicately, then you must scrape with a spatula some of the head of the penis and the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when thin, is easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very fine linen cloth, which has been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is properly performed on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest method leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not describe circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found most of the artisans and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligation with thread and incision with the fingernail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with forceps, with ligation with thread, than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times the inner layer escapes above, because the foreskin has two layers, and the inner layer remains, and is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which cobblers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not assured, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with a fingernail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with forceps and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, because the forceps are of proportional incision, for that reason the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below; and thus as you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportional, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and compression of the thread is made with a shape containing the foreskin of the penis from all sides, with which no error is made at all.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child lie down, especially if it is of those who understand a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread around its penis only, and that you leave it until the next day.
And then make it happy in every way possible for it, that which it receives in its understanding. And then make the child lie still between your hands, prepared and not moving; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them nor any other instruments.
And then put your hand to his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is revealed, and then cleanse it of whatever dirt is adhering to it.
The shape of the forceps for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be blunt and the other sharp, so that the nail head does not come out, and let the length of the two points be like the length of two thumbs equally.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the entire inner foreskin, or part of it, from below the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you stretch that [skin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it equally.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you should leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and carefully scrape it, and incise it as you see fit.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for it will harm him if you [damage it].
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum and it extends to the upper parts, this will not harm him.
And moreover, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction by a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by flesh, and similar to those things that we have mentioned in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved with what we have said and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and subtly hollowed, like a bird's quill, with a thin and long shaft, similar to a palm and a half in length, which has a slender tube in its head.
And the manner of drawing out the urine with this is that you take a doubled bladder and tie wool or cotton to its extremity with a good binding; and introduce the extremity of the bladder into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if there is any excess wool, but introduce it into the tube forcefully. And then lubricate the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub his bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
And then gently introduce the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and this will cause the patient to feel that it has reached the empty space, and it is not done according to this order, but the opening goes in a tortuous way.
And then extend the bladder with the wool little by little, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then remove it and do this for urine treatment, and use a syringe; do not cease doing this and using the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a catheter and similar instruments suitable for this.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you want to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that its extremity is blunt, that is, closed. And in that [extremity] are three holes: two on one side, like eyes.
At the beginning, there is a concave place, into which that which is to be expelled is placed, and it should be according to the quantity that fills that [concavity], without addition, so that when something is drawn from the humors by the suction, it is drawn, and when expelled, with that [same suction], it is drawn, and when it is expelled far, as a catheter does with which naphtha is sucked in naval battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw out humors from the bladder, introduce the end of the catheter into the humidity, and draw with it that which is pushed upwards in the parts, because the humidity is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then introduce the end of the catheter into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humidity with that which you expel, because that humidity arrives at the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the uppermost part should resemble a small feather, and below it a slender, hollow tube.
Then take a goatskin bladder, and place in it the liquid you wish to instill into the bladder; and then tie it tightly between two [ends] with strong double ligature, and warm that liquid over the fire.
And then introduce the end of the catheter into the penis.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it throughout its roundness; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then apply the circle so that it is sealed, after you place in it what you want of liquids, oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with that as you have done with the bladder, and express with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of a stone.
We have already mentioned in the division the types of stone and their cure with medicines, and the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and a stone generated in the bladder, and the signs of that.
And we have spoken of the stone in which one operates with the hand and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with manifest exposition and abbreviation.
Therefore I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly comes to children; and of its signs it is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries out in pain, and is restless; and with this, the anus protrudes in many of them.
And the healing of children from this is easy until they have reached fourteen years; and it is difficult to cure in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes, i.e., easier than in the elderly, harder than in children].
And of that [stone] in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., more difficult].
Therefore, concerning the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we cleanse the patient with a clyster which removes all the waste that is in his bowels; for this waste sometimes prevents the detection of the stone through examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up and be massaged and moved around so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it may move from its position sometimes.
Then make that [patient's] body between your hands prepared, and his hands should be below his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it and all that is outside.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space [bladder], then you should immediately prepare for an incision upon him.
And if it does not descend completely under your touch, then it is necessary that you lubricate your index finger of the left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if he is a grown young man, and insert it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And bring it back little by little towards the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards towards the place where you will make the incision.
And instruct the assistant to press on the bladder with her hand; and instruct another assistant to extend the genitalia upwards with her right hand, and with her other hand to hold back the perineum which is below the genitalia, in the area where the incision will be made.
And then insert a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: And incise what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger should be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the outer incision is wide and the inner incision is narrow, according to the size of the stone which it is possible to extract, and no larger, for your finger which is in the anus will easily compress the stone against the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and jagged, which makes their extraction difficult because of this.
And of those that are like a smooth acorn, and are round, the extraction is easily done.
Therefore, if it is one that is angular and jagged, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not thus expelled, then you must press upon it, or squeeze it with a suitable instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and it does not escape; or that you introduce underneath it a fine instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot succeed with that, then enlarge the opening a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then initially you place your hand at the opening of the bladder, and then incise upon it; and then push it a little further; and similarly do if there are many more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for two things may befall the patient: either death, or continuous urinary incontinence, because the area is not entirely consolidated (healed).
But administer its expulsion until it is removed, or work on breaking it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood powder, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be reduced.
Then lay the patient supine on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied it, bathe the area with water and much oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burning, corrosive abscess, similar to blood frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is prevented (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it will provoke corruption of the bladder and putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with a poultice of whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I recommended before resorting to incision, for many times because of this treatment incision has been accused; and I have already proven this, and that is that you take a smooth iron borer, of which this is the form:
of triangular sharpened end, fixed in wood.
Then take gall, and bind the penis below the stone with it so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the borer reaches the stone. And rotate the borer with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside part of the penis, because that is broken, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you for some reason that prevents it, then tie one gall below the stone, and another gall above the stone. Then incise upon the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. Then untie the ligature and clean the congealed blood which is made in the wound.
And it is not necessary to ligate with gall below the stone, except so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other ligature above, because when the gall is untied near the extraction of the stone, the urethra returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you ligate the gall above, that you elevate the urethra to the upper parts, or that it return by your action, and close the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in any of them, then its cure is difficult, and is forbidden by many methods.
One of these [methods] is because a woman very quickly becomes a virgin again.
And the second is because you will not find a woman, be she chaste or married, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is because you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, wherefore a deep incision is necessary, and in that there is danger.
But if necessity compels you to this, then it is necessary that you take a female physician who is skilled, and few such are found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a cautious and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in the matters of women, or a woman who has some understanding of this art.
Therefore, present that woman, and command her to do everything that you order, from the initial examination for the stone;  the reason is that if she is a virgin, she should insert her finger into the woman's rectum and search for the stone.
Therefore, if she finds it,  constrict it beneath her finger, and then command her to incise it.
But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the vagina of the patient and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder and compressing it with good pressure.
Therefore, if she finds it, then she should gradually draw it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts, using all her strength, until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the midpoint of the vagina above the root of the thing, wherever it is positioned, and center the stone in that area, and her finger should not be removed from the stone, holding it beneath.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that she knows the stone will be extracted from.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore, you should know how many types there are of it, so that you can signify what you wish.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the area and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And make sure you prepare with you the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the area and bind it tightly with powder and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then take care of the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the blood has subsided after a few days, and the area is dry, you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of humidity in the white scrotum, which is below the genitals containing the testicles, and is called the scrotum.
And sometimes it is in the very membrane that nature prepared for the testicle, so that it seems that there is another testicle; and this is the spermatic cord between the white scrotum that we have mentioned, and this is merely a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this humidity has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or a watery white, and this is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white scrotum which we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, tending slightly towards length, like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, for the humidity contains it on all sides.
And if the humidity is in the very membrane, then the swelling is round near the testicle, and therefore one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the humidity is between the cord of the genitals and the white scrotum, then it falls below the seam.
And if you wish to know the color of the humidity, then you test the color of the humidity in the swelling with a probe, the shape of which is known beforehand; and according to what has come out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron (cautery), then it is necessary to command the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and see his body is full (plethoric).
Then let him be supine on his back on something slightly raised, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit on his left side, and command the assistant to sit on his right, who will extend his finger to one of two sides, to the cord of the genitals and to the part of the navel.
