And then take a broad spatula and split the cord of the genitals from the middle downwards along its length almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight, equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white fat.
And peel away that [fat], and observe, and be careful not to cut into it; and your peeling with which you peel it away should be gentle, suspended and subtle, scrutinizing with the peeling as much as is possible for you.
And then pierce the water-filled cyst with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the cyst upwards; and do not touch the cord of the genitals that is included, and incise the cyst in the way that is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not carefully scrutinize its incision, there is no certainty that the water will not return.
But if the egg [testicle] protrudes from its cord during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the cyst, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the cord of the genitals with a suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is collected in two separate areas, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that area in the same way as you did the other; and if it seems to you to be one operation, then you perform it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on top more wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the wound of the belly; and place on top folded cloths and powders, and bind these on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is bound with cotton or moistened wool, and should be secured with a ligature in the waistband of the breeches, and one end should reach from the buttocks to the two [groin areas], and the second should reach to the area of the perineum, and two ends on the two thighs below, and two other ends on the two ankles, and the entire binding should be adjusted in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white cyst containing the fluid is exposed, and then you take a cautery according to this form: and it is similar to the Greek letter.
And then perforate the cyst with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then extend the cyst with forceps, and scrape it with forceps, and scrape it with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate all of it; and beware that the fire does not touch the egg [testicle].
And know that this operation is safer and involves less blood loss, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess forms contrary to expectation during incision or cauterization, or there is excessive bleeding, then you must treat whatever opposes you in this, with the treatment that suits it, as described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended here; and that is when you are certain, by what we have said concerning the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some elevated seat, then perforate the abscess at its lowest part with a broad lancet, and make the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the water runs out.
And then place a cotton cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains out. And then care for it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the outflow of water at that time, you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white panniculus in the orifice of the wound creates resistance and prevents the egress of water, then you should put into the wound a probe or instrument with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which is described before; or adjust a small opening in the compression of the wound.
And know that the water returns, and is accumulated again after about six months or thereabouts; and when it is accumulated, then let it be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its cure.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is fear leading to harm in most cases; therefore, in my view, its removal and salvation by that means is better.
And I am recalling the operation in that case and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals, which are composed of flesh, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, sharp superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion (a blow).
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the flesh, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a stony abscess formed, whose color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by the inflammation of them as it is already perceived by the recollection of it, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is caused by inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it is not fitting that one touches it.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means through compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision is made on this type [of abscess] and on the other flesh; and it is that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and tie it, and incise the suspensory ligament until you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed areas, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between some part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh at the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is completed, fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum (or, a scrotal hernia).
A scrotal hernia is an abscess enveloped by some covering similar to a sac, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is necessary to fear what preceded it (i.e., the cause of the illness).
However, we must describe the operation in the same way as the first (operation).
And I say that you must make the patient sit on a raised seat; and then gently push the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers, along with the vessels that are close to the penis; and have another assistant hold those, and stretch them vigorously.
And then incise with a broad, sharp scalpel with a transverse incision directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
And then scrape the entire area as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads that are in the temples.
And then insert a double-threaded needle into them, and tie it at the first point where you made the incision, and tie it again at the other end. And then incise them in the middle with a straight incision along the length of the body, and extract what is accumulated of fetid and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds in which you want healing to be engendered, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitals may not be injured, for that is not good.
But if you injure all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be severed along with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they be atrophied, or that they be severed so that they receive no nourishment.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen, part of the abdominal muscles, so that the intestines protrude from that rupture to some of the intestines.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or of its extension, and these two types occur for many causes:  percussion, jumping, shouting, lifting something heavy, or similar things.
And the sign of this, when it is due to the extension of the peritoneum, is that it happens gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is a swelling that gradually develops towards the front because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and there is a visible swelling below the navel, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body, through the parts outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" appears to be a term specific to this text and its meaning needs further investigation within the context of medieval medical terminology).
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this is very harmful to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness, using iron is dangerous, therefore, cauterization should be avoided in this case.
And the method of operating on this is that you instruct the patient to reduce the intestine with their hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then it should be bent over at the waist between your hands, and raise their legs.
Then hold the cord which is the spermatic cord in the upper part, and incise the spermatic cord completely lengthwise; then insert into the two lips of the incision hooks, as you need to open the incision with them.
And if the incision, according to the amount that is possible for you, allows the testicle to be expelled.
And then scrape away that layer of tissue which is beneath the spermatic cord, until the hard white tunic of each part is exposed.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into that which is between the white tunic, which is beneath the cord of the testicle, and between the second tunic, and break up with it the adhesions that are in the posterior part of the testicle.
Then gently pull the spermatic cord outwards with your hand, and with this, stretch the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic towards the incision; and instruct the assistant to extend the testicle upwards and you dissolve the adhesions behind with complete resolution.
And search with your fingers that there is no part of the intestine enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such thing, then push it back into the abdomen below.
And then incise in the part below the spermatic cord, by the incision from which blood and pus will flow, as we have recounted in what we have said.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we said is near the incision to prevent bleeding, for this often occurs), and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a windy hernia (ventosity/flatulence hernia).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done in the same way as we have recounted in the hernia that is with blood; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that side carefully; and then dry the area in the middle around the abscess with what opens it, until the vessels collapse.
And treat the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that is made in the inguinal region.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and the area swells.
And do not allow the intestines to descend into the genitals; and if they do, it is a small amount; and they return at all hours.
But if the time is prolonged with this, a fixed fissure is made in the scrotum until the intestine descends into the scrotum; and this happens because of the extension of the scrotum which is in the groin as we have said, which causes the scrotum to be stretched, and then it is softened and the area is inflamed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as has been previously described.
And sometimes it is cured with an iron according to this manner:  the patient lies on their back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with a cut of the width of about three fingers.
And then tighten the white scrotum that is under the tailbone until it is uncovered/exposed.
Then take the iron and place it on the swollen part of the scrotum, and press it towards the pubic region/the front of the abdomen.
Then sew the two prominent areas on the end of the scrotum, and join one to the other with a suture.
Then remove the end of the iron, and do not completely incise the scrotum, nor extend the cut to the testicle, or anything else like that as I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and if you have incised the tissues, clean it and restore the wound until it is healed; because the scrotum is contracted and the protrusion/hernia is not reduced.
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health/healing.
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is deceiving.
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and to incise all the swelling which is softened above the natural swelling; and then to join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew up at the beginning the excess of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
On castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of this it is incumbent on me to mention it in this book; and its mention is necessary only for two reasons. The first is so that it may be within the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, that he may know the treatment should it happen. And the second reason is that very often we have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as horses, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we should immerse the animal in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and grasp the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise each testicle with one cut until both testicles are separated.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not place any substance on those of the cyphus (presumably referring to a part of the body), except the subtle cyphus which is on the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves little substance of the testicles behind, so that the animal does not desire copulation.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where there appears in the space between the testicles, that which is between the two testicles, a woman's vulva-like shape, in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that which is in the space between the testicles.
But in women, there is one type: and it is that the woman's vulva, above the pubic area, is like two male testicles, somewhat smaller on the outer part, like a man's foreskin and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those tightened tissues until the swelling subsides, and then treat them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the space between the testicles, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A very strong swelling is caused by natural causes until it is pressed and reduced; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it is spread like in men and interferes with intercourse.
Then it is necessary that you hold back the excess swelling with your hand or with ointments and incisions.
And do not overdo incisions, especially near the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
And then treat it with wound care until it is healed.
But a born-flesh growth is flesh that grows in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and it quickly emerges outwards resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called this "tailed illness"); therefore, it is necessary that we incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of sciatica.
Sciatica is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by insufficiently formed flesh or by a thin or thick septum.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or in her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits conception and childbirth, and strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examination of the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is straightforward; but if it is not apparent, then you should look for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin septum, and is close to the two labia, then you should cut and break it up.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcefully spread the two labia until that thin septum is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and wash it with warm water daily so that the incised area does not heal.
But if the septum is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then lift it with a hook, and incise it.
And have with you the medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and incense, mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it there for a few days and apply some dry poultice.
And then it should be treated with another consolidation cure until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh [likely referring to a difficult birth or a complication], and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a phlegmonous abscess, to the phlegmonous abscess which is in the mother, no harm should be done with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and a boil or red swelling that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the openings of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when hemorrhoids are old, they become boils.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their symptoms, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the rectum of the mother and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron [i.e., cautery or surgery].
But those which are at the opening of the rectum, upon which one feels [them], are those that are treated.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cool place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at the roots.
And then sprinkle upon those wounds, to stop the flow of blood, one of the styptic powders without biting [i.e., causing further pain], such as acacia and similar ones.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have her sit in a decoction of pomegranate leaves and bark,  walnuts, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is [already] stopped, good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or comfrey juice or wallflower juice with red wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Concerning the perforation of the esophagus, an accident in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerating ones, and those that are swollen and herpetic, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types and their signs, and their treatments in the division (or section).
And it is fitting that I speak in this treatise of a burning abscess which occurs in the mother, when it is among the abscesses that collect pus. And if you wish to know in what manner the operation is performed with iron (a cautery).
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute, with percussion (possibly meaning throbbing), and phlegm, and fever, and sometimes redness, and if it affects the feeling (sensation). Then do not treat it with perforation, and cure it with that which helps maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then you must make the woman sit on a stool that has two supports; and let her recline on her back and adjust her feet to the part below her belly, and let her thighs be spread, and place her arms below her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and provides the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will come in those things that are done next.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you place with the ray (possibly referring to a light source or the direction of insertion) the cervix of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is inserted into the womb should not be more than the cervix of the womb and harms the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the cervix of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the cervix of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the cervix of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the turning device that runs in the instrument from the upper side, and that the person administering it holds the instrument and rotates the turning device until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and is touched by the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that place with a clean linen cloth.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick soaked in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the caulking in the section in the cervix of the womb.
And place outside the womb and over the vulva clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And afterwards it should be cured for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and afterwards with water in which the root of lilies or of long birthwort has been boiled, and afterwards return to the ointment.
And if the wound is deeply embedded in the closed womb, then you must be forbidden from treating it with iron, taking care of it with that which we have said in the division (section).
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner living embryos should be treated when they are expelled against nature (i.e., prematurely or abnormally).
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman straining downwards and desires to smell the air, and in addition is lightened by that which is above her from the birth, and hastens the expulsion of the embryo, then you know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the expulsion of it head first, and the afterbirth with it either suspended or hanging from the umbilicus.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo is expelled feet first and its body is expelled before its head and its feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and its hand, or its belly, and quickly is turned over upon its neck, and similar to its unfavorable forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and subtle in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be signified by this and may be established upon this.
When the fetus is expelled head first by natural exertion, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her genitals with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small spatula between her labia and tears the secundina with it, or scrapes it with her fingernail until the moisture within it is removed. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be enemas with mallow and fenugreek mucilage.
And then order her, during the enema, to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if both hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must reduce them gently little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it carefully, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is presented feet first, then you must push it upwards, and then gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one foot and extend it little by little.
But when it has emerged between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled with ease.
And if it is prevented by everything we have recounted, then take mallow mucilage and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, and then apply this to the woman's vulva, at the lowest part of her belly. And then make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to the hypochondria (the area below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts of her are softened, then make a fumigation of myrtle for her and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth shut, and lift her up, and press the lowest part of her belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
Thus [grasp] the fetus laterally, holding it with one of your hands. Try to reduce [the dislocation] with your hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and have her walk. (This likely refers to a dislocated limb, likely a shoulder or hip, where movement might help reposition it).
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her limbs with a vigorous shaking, and then administer emollient remedies, which I have already recommended (or recounted) as mucilages.
And if childbirth has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of [presumably a prolapsed organ or difficult presentation], and grasp the fetus according to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to push until it is expelled.
Thus [grasp] the fetus above the neck, spreading your hands, and its face is towards its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then gently rotate it, and extract it easily.
And when it is grasped, then place on the neck of [the baby] that which we have recommended: oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to cough gently, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean to her right side, and then instruct her to push; and make her sneeze with a [sneezing] potion. The prepared fetus exits on the side.
When the horror of the mother subsides in this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundine with your fingernail until it is emptied of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over the face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the inner parts until you place it as needed, and tell the woman to push herself a few times, and it will be expelled easily, God willing.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born, but this is rare and late in happening; but if it so happens, it is by nature and they may not live.
And although they are multiples, they share one afterbirth, and are separated by a membrane; distinguish them by the umbilical ligament of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were a single fetus by easy natural birth, and sometimes they are expelled by difficult birth as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver and lead to what we have said about unnatural positions, then treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to the position presented, she prepares the method leading to a successful outcome.
A remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten or more are formed in the mother.  It has indeed been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which was ascertained by the measurement of the glorious wise men (doctors).
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, because it quickly resembles that, and therefore she must oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it has not yet been expelled, and you are forced to operate with your hand, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted by any of the accidents which make loss of life feared for her.
Then it is necessary that the woman be placed on the bed on her side, and her head inclined downwards, and her hips raised.
And the assistant should hold her from two sides or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with humid oils and with mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the areas near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the heel, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you hold the umbilical cord with your right hand, and place its curvature in that space which is between the fingers of your left hand, and insert your hand gently and grasp the umbilical cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until you reach the empty space (presumably the vagina).
And then place and apply another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, so that there is equal traction, and there is not extension only along its straightness.  (In other words, apply multiple cords to avoid pulling only in one direction and potentially damaging the umbilical cord or fetus).
Rather, it should be moved in all directions, so that its exit is eased, just as it is done in the pulling of a tooth.
And it is necessary in this that which is between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and expertly insert them and loosen that which is retained. (This refers to the process of childbirth and easing the passage of the baby.)
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until the whole is expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow. (This describes a difficult childbirth where a hand is protruding before the baby's head and needs to be addressed surgically.)
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction during its emergence, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers expectorant spittle and split the head and extract the water, or to uncover it with an instrument that is named "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is out but constricted below the clavicle (furcula), then it should be incised until the fluid which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor resists delivery, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the fluid within drains.
But if the fetus is presenting feet first, then its extraction is easy and its direction towards the mother's birth canal is straightforward.
But if it is constricted around the abdomen and feet, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the abdomen and feet until that which is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and searches for the head with it, and draws it with her fingers towards the mother's birth canal, and then inserts into it one or two fingers, and pulls it out with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it must not be disturbed; instead, it is necessary to administer a softening of the affected parts using moist, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is lying on its side, then if possible, it should be rotated: and what we have said about a live fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that no part of the afterbirth be left inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of hot abscesses that might occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a dead fetus was, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and soon after that, the fetus died again. It happened to him that for a long time there was an abscess in his navel; and it swelled until it burst open, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied to him some plasters of strong attraction, and I drew out from him a bone, and after a few days, I drew out another bone; and I was greatly astonished, since there should not be bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted from him many bones, the woman being in her menses, and he lived in this manner for a long time, discharging a little pus.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is skill and help, that the skillful physician administers with his hand for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the lips are held together, to which constriction there are two incisions at the two ends of two poles (or rods). However, this incision should be more subtle than a constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each rod be about two fingers, and the margin of each of the two be about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two rods there are two attachments of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two attachments are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened with them when you turn the two aforementioned incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, similar in appearance to forceps. However, at its extremity there are two attachments like those you see, and the length of these attachments is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with these, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, open what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with scissors, according to the amount you want to widen the opening of the mother, until the woman does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The form of the impellent with which the fetus is expelled:
The form of the obstruction by which the large head is held back.
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps in this shape, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and held back. The form of a slight obstruction:
The form of a hook having a spine/point:
The form of a hook having two spines/points:
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed.
The form of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of those are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier in its operation and more valued by men for its quantity.
And do not neglect those instruments, whatever they may be, which you have prepared, for the necessity of them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and place herbs in it, opening the mother's mouth, such as rue, and rue, and anise, and chamomile, and dried Arabic gum, and cassia lignea, and centaury. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the uterus of the mother, then she should insert her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not collapse from the violent traction. Furthermore, it is necessary that it be moved to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mouth of the mother (cervix) is constricted, we have already recounted the treatment with sneezing and with the herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recounted, then be careful not to tire yourself in repeated attempts at extraction; however, what has come out of her at the mouth of the mother should be tied to the woman's thigh, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrapharmacon; for this will cause it to rot in a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it putrefies, a bad odor rises from it to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then it is necessary that appropriate fumigations be administered.
But some of the Ancients already used fumigation with henbane and dried figs.
Method of instrumentation with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or brass.
And the thin end should be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigant should be placed on the embers of the fire.
And keep it there until that fumigant is consumed and repeat with another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the muscle.
Then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the healing of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [presumably referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [presumably, below the surface or in a lower area of the body].
Fistulas that develop in the lowest part are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is one fistula or several.
And since that knot is ancient and discharges watery white moisture or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and flatulence; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and worms easily come out of them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space [between the buttocks] reaching the bladder, and reaching the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that is not penetrating is that you put your finger inside the anus, and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does any matter of discharge, worms, or flatulence proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then its indication is the diversion of urine from it and its prohibition, so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe enters there if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and pain arises in the surrounding area; and that there is always putrefaction from it; and that no medication is effective, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians upon which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recount to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs upwards, his thighs inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe enters.
And if the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your finger inside the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already discovered by itself penetrating, besides what you feel between your finger and that abscess or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed in some men late.
And of the medication by which help is hoped for him, it is that you ignite a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been described previously, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fistulated, fleshy growths resembling a bird's feather quill are cauterized.
And then treat that with poultices wrapped in linen until all those cauterized tissues are shed, and then treat that with medicines that generate and consolidate the flesh, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by another cure.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or abscess, and you see the fistula in that which is the surface of the gut, then cut the gut at the beginning of the fistula, and you will cut with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the tip of the probe arrives, and by the probe it is freed and expelled.
And then cleanse those fistulated tissues which resemble a bird's feather quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if a flow of blood appears between you and your operation when you incise those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is, the cauterization of those affected tissues, and the stanching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flax until the place sheds pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and drying; when then it is restored it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a membrane of fat or flesh, and it is suitable for penetration, then there is no operation there, but only a path of trust and hope.
Which cause is to be administered in three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert the poultices and the appropriate cure; and do not proceed much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that you cause harm to the patient thus without his will; and then treat it with what we have said because it will be cured very quickly; or elongate that membrane and clean it after drying it near the rectum, as we have said, with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity, and then clean those fistulous tissues which are in the section where you can with cauterization; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the rectum around the surface of the body with the curvature and in the margin of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for preparing hides in this form:
and insert it into that wound wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it is penetrating into the margin of the rectum inwards, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the rectum; and extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you incise the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues are incised which are between the two ends of the thread and they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it is very close to the peritoneum, for it is not for you to do this lest you incise the rectum; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of the pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not-sharp, so that you do not incise what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks similar to  charcoals, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are, according to the months, of little humidity, from which flows a citrine water or a little blood with continuous flow, and it is according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you order the patient to sit and to strain until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you carefully lift and hold them with hooks or with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth or with cotton or with a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of a cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey (i.e., doesn't open for) expulsion, then use a small, slightly irritating clyster on the patient, and let it be washed with that water which is in the anus and which will slightly facilitate expulsion when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture as follows: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then draw the hemorrhoids upwards, and pass the needle through the base on the other side, and wrap the two ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle, and this should be laterally; and firmly tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, except leave one unclosed, so that the excess blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place this on the anus and instruct the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moistened and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with a more forceful scraping than the first, until they bleed much and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a felon, that is, a whitlow, is nothing but a round sore, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail, and it is found throughout the body, but especially in the parts below the feet and hands, and because of these sores the pain comes.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the whitlow be incised, and held with forceps or an instrument, and eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is prescribed in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you extend and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or with a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic substance).
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated, rough eminence on the surface of the body, often appearing in the face; and when friction encounters it, a sensation similar to an ant bite is felt, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place it over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains its parts; and then rotate your hand with the cannula so that you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and place it in the depth of the flesh, and eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the application of it may be softened and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as previously described in the cauterization of athel in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to give a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or tin, or cast or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose rectums are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of its cannula, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a distinction where the bag is tied, as sometimes happens.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the rectum, should be blunt (that is, not open) but rounded, tapering to a point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the bag, in which the medicines are placed, be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its length be a palm [a hand's breadth].
And let it be perforated with many holes, so that it is woven; and let the space between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax thread, about ten threads or so; and attach the bag with that; and when the bag is placed in position, tie it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct ligatures using a strong constriction, and then cauterize each hole with that. The method of cauterizing with that is that you fill the bag with the medicine, heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place a cushion underneath him on which are placed cloths or a sheet; and then lubricate the end of the clyster with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage, or the like; and then insert it into the rectum easily, and then let the attendant take the bag and squeeze it with both hands and vigorously until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him to defecate, hold it as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you use this clyster in cases of diarrhea and his defecation is frequent, then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the rectum, then the clyster should be very fine and soft, of the kind used to administer clysters to infants.
Concerning the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the section on wound treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of these with hand and with medicines according to the circumstances.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what caused the wounds and according to the location where the wounds occurred.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are such as a fall from a height, or an incision from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the parts of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of the other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a fall from a height or similar, and only the scalp is cut and it is a large wound and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately bleed him from the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood from him according to his strength unless some cause prohibits bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced and he should not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a pledget soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been mixed.
But if there is certainty of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and the air has not altered that powder which I will soon describe briefly.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut or similar, and its lips are not approximated by themselves, then approximate those with sutures according to what I shall relate concerning the suturing of them.
But if the wound is already laid bare to the bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it but only a small suspension, then incise that and then cure the wound with medicines and restore the flesh properly in place of what is missing, and order the patient to be fed on foods in which there is thickness: heads and leg quarters of cooked fowl and similar, until it is repaired.
And if you find putrefaction in the missing part and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then cure that.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood can occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not suffice, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; therefore you dust upon them this powder, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil one part, dragon's blood two parts, unslaked lime three parts; let all be well crushed and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, that it is not festered, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone with heat, when you do not have oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they are stitched, and they are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is summer time, then this powder is not intended for application, but rather treat them with what we have said, in such a way that you apply some digestive ointments to them, or that you apply powders of barley flour with water and honey until they produce pus; and then treat them with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture in a bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to do but to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol and similar substances, because they are harmful to the nerves and constrict them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and is continuous with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as being washed with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or unperfumed oil, and  ointments when washed with fresh water and other healing herbs in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or approximate its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or there is pus in the lowest part of it, then perforate it in the most depressed place.
But if an artery has been incised in the wound and bleeding ensues, then ligate or cauterize it, or tie it off if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck have been incised, or any part, and the veins of the neck have been saved, then you should approximate the edges of the wound with sutures over the cervical spine and do not touch it; first level it and restore it to its natural form, and then bind it with a suitable dressing, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused damage to the bone, then scrape it, and if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you are unable to remove, leave it until the wound suppurates, and it is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle upon it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be mentioned in its appropriate chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then further consider if there is a whistling sound from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then in the first hour you apply powder, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart to kill the patient, but apply attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if ointment is not at hand, then you place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is contained within flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms, tremors, or a weak or constricted heart, and you see the other good dispositions of the patient, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it as previously described for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces have been broken off, then you turn the wound and attempt to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound suppurates, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds to the abdomen.
The illness which occurs in the abdomen is either great, small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it happens that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of replacing them immediately, because it opens and thickens, thus making their replacement difficult.
Therefore, the best [type of] rupture is moderate, because in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two sutures are common and are suitable for suturing other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures specific to the abdomen are also used for other wounds, but they are more appropriate for suturing the abdomen.
And I recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has emerged through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the small size of the rupture as we have said, or because the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is so, then you must warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or boiled in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is done with a liquid that has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And so, when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the reduction of it is facilitated with little effort.
But if the reduction is hindered despite this treatment, then slightly enlarge the rupture with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry it because of necessity and weakening.
Regarding the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp; the end is thin, but not as thin as a spatula, rather a little blunt.
And thus, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine is outside, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is large and is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
Similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and then have the assistant, between your pitying hands, hold the rupture entirely with their hand and adjust its lips, and then discover from that to that what suture or other cause is appropriate.
And this is the common method of suturing: first, take the needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then leave the needle at the edge of the disruption at the ring finger, and pull the needle through, but do not insert the thread into both ends of the wound, only at two ends of the part of the wound's edge beneath the thread, until you make it penetrate that part; then you have joined the two ends of the thread and two ends of the wound edges, and thus you have made four points.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle, sometimes from two sides at once, and then join the two edges of the wound with a neat joining; then leave the width of a finger and place another needle in that gap, and tie that with thread just as you did with the first needle; and continue doing this with needles as necessary until you have completed the entire closing of the wound.
And let the needles be of moderate thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles easily cut the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the wound, therefore they should be of moderate thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too deeply into both ends of the wound, lest the flesh be unduly cut and the wound open before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then, cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient during their sleep; and you shall place over the suture finely powdered substances, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those in place until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient for their suturing.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you stitch the four extremities of the wound and two ends of the lip, at once, with a needle around which is wound thread of suitable fineness and thickness.
Then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four extremities, return the needle to the same side from which you began, so that the drawn thread may fall from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture may be like a cloth suture, with which cloths are fastened.
And place between each suture the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was more than a palm, and about two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and is preserved in his faculties.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Galen. (Note:  "Paraula" is likely a misspelling or variant of "Galen".)
And it is that you begin with the suture of the quilled suture (or "suture of the quill"), and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so, as the needle penetrates the quill (or "the quilled suture") and that same laceration, according to its width and length of the whole body, leave the end of the thread into which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside at the other extremity of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again in that same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make the penetration with that [needle] with which you make that penetration in the thread at the extremity of the wound which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin on this same side and sew that [wound] with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the quill (or quilled suture) which you penetrated, and then return it into that quill and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side with this extremity of the wound; and extract it from the quill which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once and then again until you sew everything that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a suture of little skins, with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread one time from the part of the extremity of the quill, and that you leave it another time until you have finished it.
But the second proper kind of suture, which is called *Gualia* (or similar sounding name) and this is spoken of by him and by him recounted, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which certain physicians sew it, so that you match every part to its corresponding part conveniently by nature; and apply the extremity of one side and the extremity of the wound to the other extremity, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle in the extremity of the wound near you on the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that sole, and leave the two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread.
And then turn each one, and make each one penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other extremities of the wound that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the account of Gualia (or similar sounding name).
And more they told me, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, so that you introduce into these, each one, a thread, and begin with the needle introduction from what is near you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew up hearts equally.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a plague is the judgment of other plagues.
The reason is that when you treat the fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and dress it properly, then place an incarnative powder upon it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then apply some ointments which are present to you until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out, and are consolidated;  and then treat it as the healing of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines present, then immediately apply, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old linen, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you will not need other medication in many situations when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread to the principal members, then it is necessary that you submerge lamb's wool in oil of good quality, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his intestines, for many times this happens, then cauterize it with lukewarm black styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and an opening has been made penetrating into its cavity.
