And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound which falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of their magnitude, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if what proceeds from the wound is the web (likely referring to a membrane or tissue), and you find it recently produced, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already become green or black, then bind it with a bandage over the place that is blackened, so that a flow of blood may come, for in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the bandage hanging down from the part below the wound outside, so that the traction on it towards the falling of the web and emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved for you.
A reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance blow and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and formed a fistula, and from it came gas and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to treat it until it was healed and the place was consolidated.
And I, after seeing the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some accident might befall him in his belly; and no harm befell him from such an accident, and he recovered his usual strength through eating and drinking, and subsequently he bathed and exercised himself in his usual way.
And some experienced men say that when a wound occurs in the intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner: that you take ants with large heads; and then adjust the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, with its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then, when it bites and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, because it will adhere and not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then reduce it and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and it will not cause any harm to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the gut of an animal, since it is easier to use in a needle; and it is that you take the end of these gut threads, and it should be very clean, and then tie its end with the thread itself, wrapped around it, and then insert that threaded needle and sew the intestine with it, and reduce it in the disruption.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction, and not a complete healing of it.
On the treatment of fistulas and fistulae.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call it azukem.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hardened, white knot, which is painless and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and therefore it is called a knotted ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and very quickly the moisture is exuded in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is coarse, and sometimes it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their treatment with medicines, in the division (of the text).
And it is necessary that I make you know the treatment of fistula, which is done by means of an operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the limbs of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those that harm the proximity of the principal member and similar things.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and you want to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes in a straight line, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a slender lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent along the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has very horrific pus, it is not possible for you to discern it with a probe.
Then, inject a cleansing solution through its orifices, because the liquid by which you inject it goes through the other orifices and cleanses them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way it is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is distant or near, or if it is a fistula which has many orifices, and be diligent in all this with the utmost of your strength, with your questioning with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with your hand over the area, and similar signs and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all those factors with true judgment, then proceed to treatment with confidence: that is, you should consider then if it is a fistula that is near the surface or in a healthy place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from any of the places which I have described to you.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in the description of it, and remove what is in that suppuration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown in the orifice of flesh, or of cartilage, and similar things, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it as deeply as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh; and then apply heated poultices wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you must investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause involving a vein, nerve, or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow new flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the total eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and carefully perform the scraping and cleansing with all your strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that the corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which has not been reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many pieces of bone, then carefully remove all of them, and with your strength leave none of them behind.
But if some matter of the humors hinders you, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will cause the wound to suppurate, and leave it until it reaches the bone through the flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and keep the incision you made during your operation constricted.
Furthermore, if you fear, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments fester, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction are facilitated.
But if it is a large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only on the surface until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and completely incise to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, around thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his thigh.  The internal parts were inflamed with matter inside the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was the matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time passed with that [condition] due to the error of the physicians until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it.  And its treatment was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices formed in it which all discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no improvement in his condition due to a lack of skill in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter running from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was completely weakened, and his color was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and it reached the bone.
And then I promptly examined all the orifices and found them to lead from all parts of the leg.
And then I carefully incised one of those orifices until I exposed a part of the bone, and found it corrupted, and it was already rough, corrupted, and blackened; and a part of the leg was corrupted to where it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed away that which was exposed to me and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I thought that in the bone there was nothing but that corruption which I cut away and sawed, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidative medicine for a long time, and it is not healed.
And then I again investigated and uncovered the bone a second time, on top of the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me to be that corruption, and then I attempted to perform its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, in a piecemeal fashion, and attempted its restoration, and it was not restored until I had incised about a palm's breadth of it, and I extracted that with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is lightly consolidated and healed.
And it is not necessary to iterate the operation and sectioning of it except for the disposition of its weakness and for the smallness of its tolerance and its fear of death, because it happened to him at all hours from superfluity of evacuation a bad syncope.
And therefore it is healed by complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength has returned; and it is preserved in its dispositions and did not cause it contrariety in walking nor harm in any way.
But if it is a prominent bone in the place of the body already extracted, then you must saw it in this manner:  you must take a ligature and bind that to the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend it upward; and place that ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature; and then tie that over the flesh that is below the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place wood or a board decently under the bone, for when you do this it is not forbidden that the saw not cut the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupt place little by little, because if there is corruption in the concavity of the bone and it does not appear in its appearance to the touch, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupt and not prominent, before one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts entirely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupt joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it but ingenuity/skill.
And if the corruption is in the chest of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you inquire into the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and that you open that, and clean that according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each tool it should be rectified for you, when it does not oppose you vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many according to the placement of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of those, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that that operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you have need; when with you is great study and long and deep knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience of kinds of illnesses, for then you will already have for yourself that which is suitable for the instruments for every illness.
And I am forming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will posit as simple, with which you will proceed to your prohibition which you will test on others. Form of saw:

Of the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities may be affected either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause; or if you have treated that corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you should incise that limb up to the point where the corruption is located, so that the patient escapes death or a worsening condition that is a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of this is that the limb turns black as if it has been burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the corruption reaches that limb and affects the whole body; therefore, treat it with incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, such as a sea scorpion, viper, or shrew, and similar things.
And if the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it from the root of the palm, and not give space to the corruption so that it spreads into the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and to administer anything else besides that in treatment is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do this for the foot: when corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the heel of the foot, then you should incise the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him, only dismissal, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising or cauterizing a limb is this: you should tighten a ligature on the part below which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let an assistant stretch a ligature on the lower parts, and let another assistant stretch a ligature above; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures,  spreading it wide until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or cauterized.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient suffers a well-adjusted lesion and a hot abscess.
But if a flow of blood occurs while you are operating, then carefully cauterize the affected area, or place on it some powders that constrict the blood; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe before it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the corruption spreading to the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome by pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it was healed.
For a long time he suffered the same corruption in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that excess with what I applied to his hand in medicine, namely the purification of his body; and the excess was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it reached his hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and even more so fearing death from the incision of his hand, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he departed from me, he returned to his land, and it was reported to me that that man prepared and incised his entire hand, and he was healed.
And I have added this narrative only so that it may be helpful in the case of this disease, and that it may be a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus has come out, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is like thin cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and it does not impinge on bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
But however, if the space is prolonged until the corruption affects some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is that you incise that skin entirely as it is swollen, and especially when it is thinned and is like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the place through its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not reached further consequence, and the skin has thickened in appearance, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the pus from it has a foul odor, then clysterize that with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil and sharp wine and myrrh, taking equal parts, and they are mixed in a vessel, and cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with that, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with wood ash water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ash of vines, and put water over it; and then strain that and clysterize with it until you are satisfied that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the wood ash water, then clysterize with water and honey in which crushed myrrh is mixed, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has a superfluity of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and you are satisfied that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that will cause flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diachylon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with some other medicines and ointments which have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the nozzle of the clyster cannot be inserted, then you should widen it with an iron instrument little by little, and place in it a tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should approximate its lips with a suture and leave an opening large enough for the clyster to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is located high up, then dry the lowest part of it so that the pus will flow downwards, for when the pus is trapped deep within the sinus, the growth of flesh is hindered.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest point as desired, then you should ensure that you prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows easily as far as is possible, and that nothing hinders the flow of pus.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, that one you should change every hour so that the pus that collects there is removed. And so that you know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the material which is discharged onto the opening of the sinus has adhered, or is close to adhering, then on the third day you should loosen it; and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not be concerned about its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are others in whom the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has come from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying properties of the medicines. And if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their effect, they are dry, like diafinicon ointment, when the superfluous addition of zegi is adjusted in it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add old, unadulterated wine to its substance, and mix that well with it, and use that.
And it works similarly in that action, when there is no pus:  diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use that; or take myrrh, aloe, and incense, grind it all up and powder it over the honey while it's on the fire, and then apply this on a cloth and bind it tightly over the sinus; and apply this with the honey which you have cooked until thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes, aristolochia longa and celestial lily roots (and it is celestial lily) and chickpea flour and centaury are administered with it; and use these simples or compounds, grind them, sieve them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it has already infiltrated the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then you must treat it with fistula treatment, as described above.
On the treatment of paronychia, and damaged nails, and the addition of finger incisions and sectioning.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of a finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to the other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, or it is inflamed by a hot abscess, or it is corrupted, and pus is emitted until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly its corruption penetrates to the bone until there is a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you have treated this with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not work, then you must incise with an iron all the superfluous matter which remains from the nail and then cauterize the wound, because a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail being empty already compress the flesh in the underlying parts, and begin to prick and injure it, then you must place a fine saw under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards and incise that flesh easily, and apply to what remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until it is all removed; and then treat that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then you must incise that bone and remove it, for a bone which is corrupted will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the finger, then you must incise it at one of the joints as is prescribed in its treatment, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers percussion or abrasion, and experiences intense pain, then you must first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp spatula with a transverse section from above downwards, and take care that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and it will be a cause of the birth of superfluous flesh in the place; and then treat that place with whatever relieves pain.
But a finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is quickly all fleshy, and quickly becomes bony, or quickly becomes a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of a joint, or their birth is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is quickly made sore.
And of those that are born fleshy, the incision is easy, and it is that you should incise that one below its root with a broad spatula.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their joints, whether by the birth of a man born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Of the incision of vices, they are various.
Vices are various, and they are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which come in many parts of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that it be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and entirely extracted.
The incision of it is in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick blood, which is foul in the leg, is resolved, and then bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision, and then draw the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you want and which you think sufficient has been drawn, and which will take away the strength of the disease.
And then bind it up, and command him to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are swollen; or let him be exercised with vigorous exercise, if a bath is not available to you, until the limb is heated.
And then cut the vein in the direction of the vein by a longitudinal incision either behind the knee, or at the lowest part of it below the heel.
And then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein on all sides until it appears sensitive, and it is by its appearance that you will see this redness turning to blackness.
And then, when you have opened the vein, you will see that white which is like a cord.
And then you place a ribbon under it so that it is elevated and separated from the vein, and suspend it with a golden or silver hook.
And then make another incision near that incision three fingers' breadth away, and then scrape the vein from the part above the vein until it appears.
And then lift it and incise it at the back near the heel, and then extract it and lift it until it is pulled through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until all of it is out; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you in being extracted and elevated, then introduce a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or introduce a ribbon under it and rotate your hand in every direction until it is removed. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to elevate it completely, and harm comes upon the patient from that damage.
And when all of it has been removed, then place on the site of the wound wool soaked in rose wine and olive oil and un-rose-colored olive oil (plain olive oil), and cure it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is constricted, having a tortuosity which is twisted in parts, and is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it in each part of its tortuosity and in the places of its twisting, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it completely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of a sharp point so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of great flexibility, broad, because if it were thin, it would cut the vein due to its thinness; it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of a vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in Al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is easily generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, scabies, and worms are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that a vehement inflammation happens in the leg; and near the place it is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one to two drachms, tie it, and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as some cause protrudes outwards, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if the protrusion is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh, causing an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled and no part of the cause remains in the body.
And from this vein, it has emerged in some men to a length of five palms and fingers, and it has even reached me that one emerged of twenty palms.  (Note:  A palm is a unit of length, approximately 4 inches.)
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long passage to the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then cure it by the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices appear, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore cure it as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision above worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cows and is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body rising and falling, and is shown by the sensation of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and serpents and earthworms and ascarids occur in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is done only by means of its movement and appearance to the sense, and then it is necessary that you tightly bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong ligature and constriction, and then cauterize above it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies, and the greatest thing to be feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong ligature, and then cauterize above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of his body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard himself from foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease nafir (or a similar ailment).
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some members, and then is moved from member to member.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than one would fear, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and settled in the muscle, and there it remained for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I remembered that it was moving throughout her body as I had seen; and I was very surprised by its change of location and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in the woman.
And I thought that this was so because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this way the shifting wind (or air) was apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that it not appear, according to this retrospective cure, in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
And when you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place lightly and dry it until that confined wind has departed, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then you treat it with a shaking of the body and with things that expel winds, and drive them out with fetid pills and pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they fall, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some have three angles, and some have barbs; and some have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it and pierces the bone, and comes out of the cranium which is above the brain, for there happens because of it sudden vehemence, and scotoma (blindness), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there comes from the place of the wound a white humidity resembling pus, and swelling similar to the watery part of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and from the extraction of the arrow, unless it is already outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, are that it is near the left breast, and it feels as if it were fixed in something hard, and not something empty; and quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are the emission of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, and the pain then is near the lower ribs, and the breath is drawn with great pain and with sighing, and not all the members of the muscles are affected.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food emerges from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is fixed there, there will be some discharge from the wound, of tissue, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope of its cure without the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine issues forth, and some matter proceeds from it to the external parts, and severe pain comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you, what I have seen of the effects of these arrows, so that you may understand thereby your treatment.
The reason is that in one man an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of his eye, at the root of the nose, and I extracted it from him on the other side, below his ear, and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the corner of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I could grasp only a small tip with which it adhered to its shaft; it was a large arrow, one of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion-headed iron point which had no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat; it was an Arabic arrow, and this kind has barbs.  And then I incised over it between two guides, for it was submerged in his throat; and then I carefully worked on it, and I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it. And so, after thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and I pulled it out, and he was healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had healed over him, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow had fallen in her belly, and the wound had healed within her, and nothing had changed in her condition, and the woman found no impediment in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow had fallen in his face, and the wound healed and the arrow broke, finding little pain there, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the king's prince's man that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his healing about three days after the arrow's penetration.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I examined it with a thin probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was due to the tip of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant accident occurred in the area that indicated the arrow had reached that location; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and the patient desisted from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and related this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I examined it, and found the tip of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted the application of the sharp medicine to the wound until the tip of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in doing so, then I did not cease to refine and devise methods for it, until one day I managed to grasp it with specially made forceps, as the form of those will be shown at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, and I restored it and the wound was consolidated, and the patient was healed completely, and he suffered no ill effects.
And I recount to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows so that you may take this as a rule and explanation of what I have not said, for the details of this art and its definitions are not comprehensively covered in a narrative, nor do books contain them.
And skilled artisans compare the small with the large; and the present with the absent; and they find new operations and new instruments due to the descent of unusual causes in the art when they occur.
Therefore I say that arrows are not expelled from the limbs into which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction at the point of entry; or by counter-pressure from the opposite side.
And that which is expelled from the place where it entered, is an arrow lodged in fleshy tissue, and is pulled and expelled.
And if it does not readily come out at the moment of extraction, then you must leave it for some days until the flesh surrounding it softens, and its extraction and removal is eased.
And similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not readily come out, then leave it for a few days and repeat the extraction and manipulation each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not come out after a few days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a fine drill until the area around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is not palpable, then you should probe for it with a probe; and if you feel it, then extract it with instruments suitable for its removal.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should incise the wound to enlarge it so that it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held fast by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with whatever means you can; and work on this, if you cannot manage it by removing the flesh at the base of the barbs and twisting them until they yield.
And when you are performing the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use the twisting motion of your hand and forceps on all sides until it yields; and proceed with utmost delicacy so that the arrow is not broken, making its removal difficult.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then try again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then use the remedies described in the relevant chapter.
And be careful to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and employ every possible means to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, counteract the arrow so that the patient's position corresponds to the direction from which the arrow entered, because this is more beneficial. But if that is not possible, then use whatever means are available to you, according to the circumstances.
But if arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of the arrow projects outward from the wound, or if the tip of the arrow causes swelling due to the feeling of the part above the deep wound and you see this swelling; then you should incise there; and the incision should be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will come out easily in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrow is loosened from the bone, and widen the opening, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow remains inside, then push it in further.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer a push, then insert, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity fits over the tail of the arrow and then push it in.
But if the arrow is concave, then push it in with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrow is thereby eased.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you must remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls into the foot, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut over it, then cut over it and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Let their ends be like the beak of a bird, made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the constriction of the area.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy (bloodletting) of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head:  two pulsating veins near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins in the two apparent temples; and two veins in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi (possibly a type of vein) that are at the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these, one is the cephalic, and it is on the ulnar side (little finger side); and the common people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a superficial vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilica, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name that one "vein of the whole body", and the basilica is a vein which is located on the radial side (thumb side), and is named "citillar", and the common people call that one "vein of the belly"; and the funis of the arm, and it is located on the fold (of the elbow), and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the fold by manifest appearance; and the salvatella, and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger, and it has two branches.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is below the bend of the knee on the ulnar side; and the second is the saphenous, and its location is below the leg on the radial side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the ulnar side; and in the leg are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for old cataracts, migraine,  sahanfati (a disease, likely requiring further research to identify), and old, bad ulcers of the head.
And the method of bleeding these veins is as I recount to you:  that the head of the patient is raised, and the head of the patient is rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth, with good friction; then the patient tightens his neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears, in two depressed places of the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of these with your finger, then bleed them with a lancet.
Then you take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the blood flows out. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the width of two fingers joined, or thereabouts; and let blood flow as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already, at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization has been described.
But for the two arteries in the temples, bloodletting is beneficial for chronic migraine, sudden severe headaches, persistent ophthalmia (eye inflammation), and acute effusions of superfluities into the eyes.
And the method of phlebotomizing these is as I have recounted to you: constrict the patient's neck tightly until the two veins appear visibly to the touch, and their pulse is felt under your finger; and then you may cut with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet underneath, raising the vein upwards; and incise it in the same way as you did the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the ligature on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it tightly above, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and the traction have been discussed at the beginning of the book.
Therefore, phlebotomy of the frontal vein is appropriate for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein in old facial diseases, and for migraine, stiffness, and foul ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recount to you; that is, that the patient constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a fleam, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fleam upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or other suitable object in the manner of it, and let the blood flow according to the quantity that you wish; and then loosen the constriction on the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of a sharp point as are the other phlebotomies, rather let it be of a flat point, and perform this phlebotomy transversely, because the bone is close and it would easily break the phlebotomy instrument when it is thin.
But the treatment of the two veins that are at the tear ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for stiffness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize there, and you stand above his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transversely rather than lengthwise, with a small phlebotomy instrument, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy instrument is thin, its point is easily broken.
And then allow the blood to flow as much as is necessary to you, and place cotton there, and bind it for a night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and stiffness that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin phlebotomy instrument and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge straight, because the vein is not palpable there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.

Concerning cupping and the qualities of its use.
Cups are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cups that are administered with scarification and bloodletting, there are fourteen locations on the body, of which the one where cups are placed is called *alnocrati*, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle location between the two sides of the neck together; and cups on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cups on the two shoulder blades; and cups on the *alohoster* (possibly referring to a specific anatomical location, perhaps the lower back or hip), and it is above the end of the coccyx; and cups on the two *focils* (likely referring to some anatomical point, perhaps the thighs), and they are on the two thighs; and cups on the two *cavilhas* (possibly the knees or ankles), and cups on the two hips.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not deplete strength through them, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping for illness or for a continuing condition, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of these conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves the heaviness in the head and that which is felt in the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on someone with a cold constitution or who has a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore it is not advisable to use this on the elderly, and on those whose heads suffer from cold ailments.
But to one upon whom this is continually done, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you must order the cupper to place his hand a little below the cupping area, to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping in this place is in place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the windpipe, and the tearing of the windpipe, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the cupping glass in this place be slightly elevated, for if it is placed too low, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping at al-hagdahan benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you must order the cupper not to press his hand on the scarification so that he does not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases of the mouth, and stands in place of phlebotomy of the arteries that are in the two lips.
But cupping that is done over the two shoulder blades benefits caphalgia, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping of the two inner parts of the ankles benefits in the same way as phlebotomy of three veins, that is, of the basilic, median cubital, and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And command the cupper that he not proceed too much in scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping-glass with which one cups over the hips and it benefits the hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that it be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly broken and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain by evident depletion, for it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits boils and swellings; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two jugular veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And of the jugular cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the place by equalizing pressure. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of the blood appear.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction little by little, and consider the condition of the body.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification and make it a little deeper, and moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its watery part. But in the second [scarification], to the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the latter [desired effect].
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with deep scarifications.
And the term "scarification" in which [it is] only the depth of the [scarification] needle.
And if the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for the cupping glasses be anointed with lenitive, aperitive, and resolvent ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or St. John's Wort oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then let the oil be amaranth oil, or balm oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar to them.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be forbidden, and for such a person the places for cupping should be washed with warm water, or with cold water of nightshade, or with poppy water, or with  water of  borage, and similar to those.
And he whose blood is of excessive humidity, wash his cupping sites with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar to those.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with water of bugloss (or possibly, given the context, majoram), or of anise decoction, or of chamomile, and similar to those.
And the cupping should be performed before the bath; however, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and the patient should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled ought to observe before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has hot blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sour wine and lettuce, and succory, and julep, and things similar to these.
And give him foods such as chicken, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and similar things.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of succory seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine, a middle course between old and new; and it should be recommended to him that he use moderation in drinking and eating, and give him foods such as chicken,  hempseed,  peas, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, his drink should be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain hours some men be given theriac of alpharaz, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or celandine before cupping or before bloodletting or afterward, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood thinned; and they should not take those things heated.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the urinary bladder, because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And it is not desired for those [members] except the attraction of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of nasal bleeding; and we use this so that cold, invisible flatulence in the member may be dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and the navel; for this rarefies the member and warms it and diminishes the pain because it resolves flatulence.
And it is placed on boils when they occur with obstruction or swelling, for by virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of flatulence enclosed within them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Form of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have formed it; and let the quantity of the cup be half a palm; and let it be on the side of him, in the place that is in the circumference, half of him, let the hole be small according to the quantity that a needle enters into it.
Let it be made of brass, copper, or tin, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb because of its position on it.
And let there be a transverse bar of brass or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have mentioned; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and the weak are different from the cupping glasses for youths and those with strong physiques.
And the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend a wick with a decent wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass is immediately dissolved/removed.
And then ignite the wick according to its nature, and repeat if you need to.
But in cupping that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and it is not administered except that it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb only.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible because of their small size, such as the fingers and gums and similar; or on a limb devoid of flesh, such as a finger and nose and the like.
And the quality of using those leeches is that you have found leeches that are in clean, sweet waters free of putrefaction.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no matter remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then let them be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to suck the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged after the application of leeches, and it oozes, then you dip a cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the bleeding stops; or place halves of roasted beans on the area, and leave them until the beans adhere to the area and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to iterate the application of leeches, when it is possible to change them.
And if leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then apply them, for they will readily attach when they sense blood.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle on them a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash, and they will detach immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish physicians and commoners boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter of them.
And for this reason, the manner of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at any time; and I have not added to what I learned, except by long reading of the books of the Ancients and study to understand them, until I extracted the science from them.
And I have added to it through experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and on which my experience is based; since I have abridged it for you and freed it from lengthy elaboration; and I have abridged it to the point of brevity; and I have shown it clearly; and I have illustrated it with many forms of instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of added explanation, as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collections on the subject of bone fractures, those things which are necessary to preface before we begin the discussion of broken bones and the various aspects of each. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the main points of the discourse; and the chapters on which the craftsman must initially focus and stand firm on the truth of these things; that is, you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art more than others among you.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall occurs to someone, then you should begin with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness, or the person who is injured is an infant, or an elderly person in a weakened state, or it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And furthermore, let him be content with a diet of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and prohibit fatty meats and filling foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a good healing, and you do not fear effusion of matter into the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regimen, to which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, rich and thick and heavy in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat boiled in water, and rice, and heads [perhaps referring to specific cuts of meat], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things, and with this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the elderly, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature fits and brings to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are together glued and tied together, until they reach the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until no cause prevents it from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that you provide the patient with foods in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Many diversities of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture, besides what happens in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not-proceeding, as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs its own method or torment in its restoration, as will be recalled distinctly in its proper place.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this is a bone fracture known:  its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that which we feel when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is this felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a crookedness or small fissure, where it is not fitting that you move it with your hand and with complete compression; however, place upon it the medicines of which mention will be made in their proper place; and then it should be tightened by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is bent but not split in two, or other such thing does not happen to it except that it is bruised in some part of its entirety, then it is necessary that you begin immediately to rectify it before an abscess forms there.
And if a hot abscess forms there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then dress it according to whatever light and ingenious method you can.
And know that the restoration and healing of this is easier than of a bone in which a wound has occurred.
And then bind it as the subsequent instructions will indicate.
But if the bone is injured, then extension of that broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And your position should be such that you place the injured limb in a bandage according to its natural shape, while the broken parts of the limb are extended; then you must, with skill and in whatever way you can, return those dislocated parts to their places.
And take care, according to your strength, that neither pain nor injury befall the patient through your operation; and you must, according to your ability, apply one bone to its fellow, according to their best alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that with your hand, and if you see anything amiss there, rectify it and correct it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do, who often cause a hot abscess through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operations many times.
And the quality of the binding of the calmed limb is that which I recommend; so that you may know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their size and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the forearm and similar to those, then the wrapping should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, the wrapping should be broad and firm, because a broad bandage connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish enters into it.
Then begin, after your equalization, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then come with that to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did with the bandage with which you bound the fracture site. Then extend the bandage from the fracture site a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you have covered a large part of the area.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken area, and then bring the wrappings to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your action in tightening the bandage and in softening it be as we have said in the first wrapping above.
Then place between the layers of the bandage linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is any crookedness; and if not, place nothing there.
Then place another bandage over it, and then carefully apply properly made splints over these bandages, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area; and then place on it whatever draws out the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be made of half a reed, smooth and skillfully shaped; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this shape and this figure:
