However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint should be according to the size of the limb; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread properly as we have said concerning tightening, and it should be that your tightening is greatest at the location of the fracture, and the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the practice of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, it is a great error, when the tightening lacks uniformity.
And similarly, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with them what you want through tightening, and there should not be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the ends of the splints near the bandages in those places, then place under those ends some carded flax or wool until that does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will return to this in the following chapter according to its particularities.
And know that not every broken limb is splinted on the first day; the reason is that, when the limb is large, you should not apply splints until about five or seven days, or more, according to your assessment of the risk of a hot abscess forming.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients used to treat, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of common plaster, a shortened treatment for fractures, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, because it is of a substance which neither heat nor cold overcomes: it is to take the powder of the moli, which is the pith of the flour that settles on the walls of the mill during its operation.
And prepare it in this way, except that you sieve it with egg white, and make the confection neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for treating fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of migrach (which is the root of wild pomegranate), of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms; of aloes wood, twenty drachms; all should be crushed and sieved and mixed with water of aloes wood or with egg white if the patient's complexion is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and treats lightly broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster which is good for fractures and sprains: take of migrach and white marshmallow root, ten drachms; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms; of acacia, six drachms; of Armenian bole, twenty drachms.
All should be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and be sieved, and be made into a confection with water or with egg white and be administered.
A method for plastering joints and bones separated from their places, and for pain occurring in them, and for fissures and fractures: take greasy wool and submerge it in sour wine and cooked oil and place it on the area.
However, this plaster does not have restorative virtue; it rather draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound everything and make a plaster from them.
A method for a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of pea flour, of each an equal part; let all be crushed and be made into a confection with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then make this confection with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method for a plaster stronger in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and of pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each an equal part; let all be crushed and be made into a confection with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
But in Wales it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which are drying with little heat are thus, such as myrrh, incense, and similar things to those which we have mentioned.
Method of a plaster which benefits injury and pain: take lint,  beeswax, shaved human hair or bird down, and marshmallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and cooked, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time for which the bandage should remain and then be loosened is that you consider if no pain or itching has occurred and the bone is not broken or dislocated, then you do not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should carefully loosen or remove it at once, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moistened sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with it wash the area until that itching ceases and the pain is soothed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place upon it clean wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided, and its pain is relieved.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it with your first constriction, but refine it with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have now completely subsided, and you feel a need for a plaster and binding, then you repeat it in the same way as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occurs in the limb, then do not loosen it until after three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you by the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as its recovery has preceded.
But if you see the site of the fracture drying out and thinning more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore bathe it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation, nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning as it should be, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their operation, and a great risk; and if it were properly done, the Ancients would have magnified this in their books and would have worked with this.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you should not do it.
Chapter Second: Of a fracture when it occurs in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by a blow from a sword, and the blow crushes the entire bone into the marrow, which is beneath the bone, just as it does in the shaft of a tree; and because of this, this type is called *ascial* (axial?); or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its flesh, but the incision does not reach to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation/lifting?).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of a fracture that is comminution or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, so that it approaches the periosteum which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as small as the thinness of a hair, and because of this, this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow from a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, just as happens to the plates of brass when a blow is struck and part of them is driven into the inner parts.
And this happens especially in the bodies of moist bones, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will bring all of this back to its place.
And we shall know all these types of fracture by detection on those and by inquiry of them with probes and removal of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is discovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and probed with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the patient's symptoms, and then if you see by the symptoms that indicate by manifest signification danger, such as vomiting of bile, and extension [possibly referring to limb extension or spreading of the injury], and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and rolling of the eyes and rigidity of those, and similar symptoms in the worst cases, without doubt it is not healed.
And if you see no unusual symptoms, you expect recovery, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the wounded one has come to you at the beginning as wounded and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you take care to set the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus [likely referring to the soft tissue], and before the symptoms that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicle covering the brain, and is with small and pulverized fragments, then it is necessary that you incise the comminuted and pulverized part, as I will explain to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the wounded patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the shape of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if you encounter, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin with the circumambulation of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incision knife, and this is its shape:  And then use another incision knife, a little wider than that one; and this is its shape:
And then use a wider incision knife than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incision knives, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and let them be sharpened at the ends, and be made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them easily in percussion on the incision to prevent you from hitting the head and injuring it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you use the incision knives with the drills, which are called non-penetrating drills.
And they are not called thus except because these do not reach the bone of the skull to that which is near it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is below its sharp head, similar to an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, so that you have present in all the parts of the skull an end which is sharp, the length and shortness being according to the amount of thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; then move the drill to another location, and place the space between each hole at the size of the thickness of a finger, or thereabouts.
And then incise with scalpels that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can, for the sake of lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other instrument which you will have prepared for this, such as small pincers and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the scalpel does not touch anything of the brain.
And so, when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth its roughness, that which is in the bone, with another instrument similar to a scalpel, but however it must be thinner and more subtle than the other scalpels.
And if some small and splintered bone fragments remain, then take them away easily with whatever instrument you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, far from fear, which Galien described and praised for its great usefulness; and this is his description: it is necessary for you at the beginning that you begin to uncover the part of the bone at the place where the fracture is most vehement and most manifest, until, when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the tip of this slow and gentle scalpel of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is broad and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided in two edges forward in length, like you see; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain, with its face, the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
And then strike the scalpel on one side with a small mallet until you cut through all the bone easily as it is revolved, and you are sure of the panniculus that no accident will occur to it in any way, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Then if some material remains attached to the bone of the panniculus in any part of the bone, cut it away with the lenticular end of that scalpel, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculus, and what is severed or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain, then you must scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps for which you have prepared a number, of various sizes, so that it is possible for you to use in each place those most suitable and those most appropriate according to what guides your operation on that broken bone and its form.
And you must proceed in your scraping, with which you scrape the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then gradually use the finer ones, and do not cease doing so continuously until you come to use the finest and most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other small, hairline fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what leads you to their rectification.
And this is a matter not to be hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And I stop here on what we have shown in writing concerning large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you scrape the bone, then you must take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it over the thin cloth, doing this as gently as possible, so as not to irritate the tissue; and then apply a broad bandage over it, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only.
And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are certain of a hot abscess; and then untie it and then apply medicines which counteract excessive dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, and powder of enses [likely a plant, possibly henbane or similar] and birthwort, and things similar to these.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and universally, apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite (or irritate).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and let there be no filth of ointment or other things in it; nor allow virulence to gather there; for when it is applied to the pannicle of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and thereby causes grave harm to the patient.
And it affects the brain tissue of the head, as the bone is cleaned and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, consider that if the patient suffers the accidents we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if the blackness is not caused by the medicine placed on it, but is by the virtue of that medicine that causes that blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and then place it on the brain; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two of its most superior parts together, or one of them sometimes, because it has two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and it does not suffer from it except attrition and crookedness and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into their nose, and that you should align that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and beware that you do not cause pain to the patient by your operation.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a small, thin rod.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do likewise.
And strive to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately on the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
Then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or place two small wedges, if the fracture is in two parts; and let the wedge be of such a size as to fill the opening of the nose.
And I say that some of the ancient restorers [surgeons]  advised that the wedges be dipped in a broth and changed daily, but I have not found this to be effective; rather, it is necessary that the roll [of linen] be dipped in egg white mixed with powdered millstone [or finely ground stone]; and then leave the wedge in place until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, may be inserted into the nose; and this is especially useful if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this [use the feather], and if you wish, do the other [use wedges].
And if, on another occasion, a hot abscess occurs,  dress the nose with cerate [a medicated ointment] or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon [a lead-based plaster].
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you should dress it externally with sesame flour and powdered lentils mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted [shattered], then you must incise over them and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then close the incision and treat it with things that promote granulation and consolidation, and with suitable plasters for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you must treat it with wedges and administer lead canulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the broken parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with that the convexity of the fracture inwards, easily aligning the broken parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus decently equalizing it.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two parts according to the straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the broken jaw a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place on the cloth a suitably made large compress, or a piece of linen equal to the length of the mandible; and then bind that above according to how the bandage is prepared for you and as the application requires, until it is healed.
And instruct the patient to use rest and quiet, and give him food of sorbitive lions. (This likely means "easily digestible foods").
And if you estimate that the shape is already altered in some way, then you should observe and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what is already altered and apply a plaster with fine powder, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the cerate, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and fitted to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not unbind it until it is healed and holds the fracture; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and let that abscess be softened/matured.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam, according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should sew up the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and that will help it to heal, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part, below the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds and aligns the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that side.
And then bind the fracture with your bandages until its shape is properly restored, and there is neither a small prominence nor a concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, its size according to your needs; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts because it is stuck to the sternum, then the patient should be supine on his neck, and you should place a moderately sized cushion on his muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is on the sternum above is elevated, and then you straighten the fracture and bind it with your bandages.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bad, then you should incise over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you carefully make an incision with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves the purpose; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is its form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends like this, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you should join the two edges of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And ensure that the splint is not loosened from the broken bone; and for that it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so that when the bandage is loosened or you see the splint is already displaced, then you rectify this and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their heel at night; so that with this the limb is elevated from the bed, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated from the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless a boil or abscess occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the constriction, and restore it and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the clavicle is consolidated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the case, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its lateral side, and not shattered, only the edges are broken.
And so when the place is broken—and it is broken in the middle of the place—and is not known except by touch, then, according to what the shape of the fracture is, force the preparation of it and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered finely ground albumen with lion's wool; and then place a poultice of doubled cloth; and then place over it a smooth, thin linen bandage, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage shows a concavity in any place on the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then apply the bandage evenly.
Then bind over it with a long bandage with decent tightness and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time that it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and straighten the bandage if it is moved from its place.
And let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And so when this number is complete, then you unbind the bandage, and you should know for sure that this is from the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the collarbone and extends under the armpit, then you make an incision over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the treatment of a fracture of the clavicle or of a hot abscess if that occurs there.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster, as they are scarred and comminuted.
And among its accidents, when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines inward, causing great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and strong fever and vomiting of blood; and it is evident in the cavity of the broken bone and is not hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and adjust the broken bones with hands from both sides, and be skillful in the operation and in the setting, in every way that is possible and appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place over it a plaster and lint, and over that a splint of thin willow or alder wood, or similar light materials, after you wrap it in cloth; and be careful in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not loose; and use a circular bandage around the dorsum sometimes, and tighten it with a decent constriction; and then check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it becomes loose.
And if necessity demands loosening it because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should examine and unbind it, remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred by means of its appropriate treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if it seems appropriate; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only happens in the thicker parts, where there are two; and in their extremities at the front part, this crushing does not occur, because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from sensation through its examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus: equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as needed, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and he experiences difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were ingenious in that with many inventions.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we should avoid this so that it does not easily cause a hot abscess if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it already helps that.
And some of them say that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw with force; and this seems reasonable, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass will attract superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them say: the place should be covered with wool that has been immersed in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is even as it enwraps that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy of food and medicine.
And if a violent cause constricts the patient, which is unbearable, and the bone pierces the skin with a painful puncture,  we fear for the patient, then it is necessary that we make an incision over the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument that preserves the *ciphac* (a word I cannot define with certainty - it might refer to a specific surgical tool or anatomical feature), the remembrance of which is previously mentioned, and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an internal abscess occurs, then you should take and infuse powders in rose oil and place it on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it is slow to heal, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider: for if you see their hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor flex them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if he has something similar in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, the same thing has happened to them as happened in the hands, and when he lies on his back, he has flatulence and bowel movements without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those things happen, the cause is better and lighter.
And the cure for him is that you should apply to the maturation of the hot abscess, that you place on the affected vertebra,  pure rose oil, or with boiled egg yolks;  apply some of this several times a day until the hot abscess is matured.
And then apply one of the comforting and drawing plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he not sleep on the side where he finds pain until he is healed.
And if it happens because of the attrition of the bone, a splinter, or some cause that is already separated from it, then you must dry the wound and remove that bone; and then bring together the two edges of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the lower part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the hip joint, then you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and reduce the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible by counter-traction; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you need them, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel splinters broken in it, then you should probe for them, and remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then the fracture is only a separation at the ends of those bones and is lengthwise, and inclined towards the inner parts; and the patient experiences pain and numbness in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is prepared for you by equal binding until it is similar to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or it inclines towards the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach so that the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then, when you have bandaged it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or reed over that, and tighten it with a bandage over which you do not fear a puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And bind the concave part with something that fills it until it receives the bandage equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is sedated, and place on it that which sedates it as is preceded; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens in the bones that they are pierced or comminuted, then you extract them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone is that which is between the neck and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at its two extremities, and then let it be suspended from an elevated position.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then place his broken arm upon the splint until it reaches his little finger, in the middle of which the curve of the splint should be placed.
And then suspend some heavy weight from it, or let the assistant extend it downwards; and then let the physician set the fracture with his hands, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on his back and suspends his hand from his neck with a bandage; and then order two assistants, that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the lower part; and each of them extend to their part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten above the fracture with a bandage and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants extend to their two parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the little finger and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and on that elbow.
Swallow it easily and without effort, and then, when it has been swallowed as it should be and is applied by good application, tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the constriction until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand on the muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is necessary to place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints follow the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is necessary that your bandage over the fracture site be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you have done it correctly.
And if you fear a purulent abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then observe the bandage in all parts for three days so that neither itching nor inflammation occurs at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb because of the tightness of the bandage; and therefore rectify all this according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleeping position be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place on the splint a poultice full of wool, carefully, and check every hour of the night and day that the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; therefore, rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until the tightening begins; and then it is necessary to thicken his food.
Because it is customary for the splint and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound and a bath be administered and it be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is widely comminuted, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints until 10 days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focil is broken alone, or the smaller, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the focil is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient for the physician, before its restoration, that he apply slight extension easily until it is aligned.
But if the large focil is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both focils are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place your hand during its restoration and its extension stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of your hand should be on the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a square-shaped pillow.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer any strain.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
And then the physician should set the bone until he reduces it to the best position he can.
And if the bone fracture is complicated, then you must forcefully reduce all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated complication appears in him, that the bone protrudes and there is no hope in its restoration, then you should operate on him and remove it according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dedicated a special chapter to that, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of rose oil and white wax, of a consistency midway between thick and thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a poultice prepared from powdered barley flour with egg white, and then apply a compress; and the compress which is placed over the fracture should be thicker and stronger.
And know that the number of compresses for the arm depends on the severity of the situation, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site firmly and tightly, and if the bandage is applied to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the bandage a little more loosely, as was explained previously at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of a moderate thickness, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have previously recommended.
And examine the limb and its binding after a few days. And if any complication arises that requires correction, such as itching which may occur in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until the itching subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is reset and similar to the others, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by an excessively tight bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it thus for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient suffers from none of the conditions we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended by its own agitated movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the disposition of the vital force/healing power.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the wrist and finger joints; instead, what often happens is attrition (wear and tear).
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down, and a cushion should be placed beneath him, keeping it level, and then place his hand on that [cushion] extended [outward].
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them until they are joined with a good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and wrap it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to grasp it with his fractured hand; then it should be bound tightly with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather may be applied to the palm; and bind it as is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary that you place a bandage above and another below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, in such a way that the hand is bound to the splint between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in one of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, apply a small, straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be straightened and bound to the finger next to it; or all should be bound together consecutively, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and next to it, the disposition of hot abscesses and oppose — with your operation — what is necessary when any of those things occur, according to the repeated narration of them.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and this is revealed by the sensation as it is displaced forwards and upwards.
And its restoration is done in this way: a ligature is tightened above the fracture and another ligature below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the assistant extends all the ligatures to their proper place according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then the ligature should be close to the knee at the hamstring area, so that the extension pulls upwards, and the other ligature below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then the ligature should be placed close to the knee, and the extension applied to the parts below.
Then the physician joins the bone with both his hands until he reduces it to its natural form and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the heat of the abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made like this:  You should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the strip come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is firm.
And then begin with a tight bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four turns, and the farther you move away from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the cloth to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tie both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage does not shift from its place.
And then leave the bandage on it, provided that it does not cause the limb to become corroded, or develop a hot abscess, or swelling, or anything similar; and if any of these things happen, then immediately loosen it, and rectify whatever has happened as soon as possible, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone which protrudes, then you must reposition it as best you can; and if not,  cover it and leave it, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened in addition to the splints being applied, just as we have said in the treatment of the arm.
But however, in the restoration made in this way, lameness does not befall the patient; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being supported, then it is necessary that he will limp constantly.
And know that the thigh is bound for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions (of the patient).
Chapter 15.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but attrition often happens to it.
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and everything about this has been examined. (or "...and this is with or without a wound, and this is all determined by examination.")
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts is aligned with the said [bones/parts] until it is joined and conjoined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication. (The "fabrication" likely refers to setting and splinting/bandaging.)
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the provisions that we have recommended in other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that all accidents are countered with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is called by the name of tibia (shinbone); and the other is slender and is called fibula.
And the same types of fractures occur to these two bones of the leg as occur to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the more slender bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the larger bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is among those things that are not easily healed.
Then administer extension, equalization, and bandaging of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is extensive, having many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in a leg injury, the procedure is similar to that of the arm. When you align the splints and complete your operation, you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the parts that are placed on the soles, and they are made of palm branches; choose those of small thickness, not the small ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap each one in two cloths, according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, extending from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle.  For with this structure the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and it remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been prepared; and let the leg be placed on it so that it preserves it from movement.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound along with it.
Then examine the leg each day and study it carefully, and watch for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems; and if any of these occur, then attend to it with whatever is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and of the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the entire talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do sometimes occur; and fractures of the toes occur rarely, and they suffer more from attrition in cases of improper positioning.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and these bones are raised up between themselves, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on top of the elevated bones, and then tread down on them, pressing and pushing them until they return to their proper places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place a small board under the sole of the foot, to which two pads are attached.
Then bind that tightly to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
And when three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones set, whether they were broken or dislocated, and the operation in these cases is as you know.
But if some say they are broken, then restore them and set them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then apply a splint to the broken finger according to its length, and let it be a little longer; and place under the foot that board which I have described to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in lion cloth, and tighten the board at the base of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board above the belly of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you not forget to consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is suitable.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is broken, then make her lie in a low lying position, and gently incline her to one side, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be held, and her hip raised little by little, for all this is useless until the broken part of the hip is in the shape of a ball and the broken bone is returned [to its place].
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reduce and return it according to the manner in which you set it at the beginning, and then return to the first powdering.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and introduce the whole bladder into her hip, and then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will return the fracture [to its proper place].
And then let it be filled with cotton and remain so for several days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recounted for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these proximate fractures which happen infrequently, especially to one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture signifies to you in the greatest dispositions the right way in its restoration and in its binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the penis of a man, when it is broken, take a hen's gizzard, and place the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to those, that you take care and bleed him immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy testify to you as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to its retention so that you sprinkle crushed egg yolk on it, if no other cause is present to you.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong this, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin uncovered, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight fingers and whose width is according to the size of the wound; and for this, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the amount needed for this in the treatment of every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp point with a margin at its ends; and let the upper part of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that its sharp end be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and it is held with some bandage.
And then strive to bind the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not receive the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recounted, then incise it with whatever is appropriate from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement pain remaining, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do this because you will confer great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound a cerate or other substance containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption there.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or festering illness, that is, gangrene, upon their patients. And if your constriction is light, soften it, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accident among those we have recounted, and you find pain in the area, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to generate pus, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then administer balms, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it behooves you to untie the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows down from it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass and the wound is not consolidated nor is the pus removed from it, then know that here are the beginnings of little worms, for which it is necessary to probe the wound with a probe. And of those beginnings, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those that are not separated and cause pain in the limb, force yourself to cut them, as far as it is possible for you.
And if there occurs in the wound a swelling or ulceration—that is, an abscess—or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate to it from the cures of which the narration precedes in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should dwell upon, and you should attend to this matter: when a large bone is broken,  bleed above the limb, just as with the bone of the thigh and similar large limb bones, and do not attempt traction or extraction from it, for in many instances death results from this; rather, leave it until it festers, for it will soon fall away by itself in about twenty or thirty days; and then treat the wound if it seems appropriate to you, but if not, leave it be.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of healing of a fracture, especially if it is near the joints, and the shape of the limb is distorted by it; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerussa (white lead), and similar substances; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a cloth, and bind it firmly in place.
And leave the bandage tight; do not loosen it for many days; and then loosen it and reapply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a lead plate over it gently, for lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already hardened and become constricted, and necessity requires its removal carefully, then incise it in its most superior part, and incise the superfluous and prominent part, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set, and the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is the multitude of solutions of the ligaments, and its binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightening of the bandages, such that nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or by excessive and superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and make his heart cheerful so that his blood is multiplied; and administer baths; and instill joy and gladness into him.
And then apply a plaster to the limb, so that the plaster draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and the limb returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, after a limb has been set, there is a crookedness and swelling at the fractured bone and a deformity of the limb's shape, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the opinion of someone who claims that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy, leading to great fear, whose sign is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be prepared and mixed with white clay or wax oil, and applied as a plaster; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar medicines that are called consolidating emollients.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb in all directions should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become hardened and turned to bone, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron, then the uppermost part should be cut away and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells beyond the bone should be incised with fine instruments, and relief should be given to it with care and skill.
Chapter 23.
Concerning discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, wherefore movement is prevented, and the shape of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to strive immediately that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly the reduction of the dislocation becomes difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and let it be until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will bring it back to its proper place (or function).
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what precedes in fracture; from the most superior part of the body down to the most inferior part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it is not delayed; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways:  they are moved from their places by slight movement, and are somewhat softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, to the point that they are softened at the joint, to the point that saliva flows from the patient and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is entangled in his speech.
But when his dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little help.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then a slight reduction must be administered, and it should not be delayed at all.
And it is that the assistant holds the patient's head, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax his jaws and release them from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it powders of cloth with cerate, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and then let it be tied easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be positioned between two pillows, so that it does not hurt him on the right or left side; and do not force him to chew anything; before his meal let his food be finely chopped, until when the pains are relieved and the jaws are knit together, then let him eat what he wishes.
And this should be administered easily, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is knit together and healed.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and constant thirst occur, and very quickly the patient's belly is distended, and very quickly he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times he who this happens to dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards by its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lie on his back and extend his arms. And then compress the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powders to that area and bandage it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it must be reduced and bandaged according to what we have said and according to what is at your disposal.
And then apply a plaster, and powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes to the lower parts below the nipple, and especially in those who have little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those who have much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought to be dislocated because of this.
Then it is necessary that you prove this before you are certain of it, and then you proceed to its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the nipple; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity, and below the nipple, near the touch of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it through all movements.
If it is dislocated near the feet or above, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And of this dislocation, reduction is easy when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below the patient's nipple, and raises the joint strongly upwards to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and much time has passed since the dislocation first occurred, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and an emollient and softening liniment should be applied, such as boiled roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be administered.
And then the patient should lie supine on his back and a wool pad, of a consistency between soft and firm, should be placed under his nipple. Then the physician should place his heel on the pad and raise the head of the muscle forcefully, and pull the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, for then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the affected side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit and lift the patient's armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's arm from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with him to add weight, so that it will weigh him down, for the dislocation will immediately return.
And it is reduced thus according to another method: a long pole is fixed in the ground, the head of which is round in shape, like a pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; when sheets are placed on top of the pole, and the patient is standing along the length of the pole, and then their arm is extended downwards on the opposite side; and their body is pulled forcefully on the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a pole two cubits long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be reduced near the armpit in this manner:
And then, on the rounded end, soft cloths should be tied so that the pole does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the armpit of the patient, and the arm or hand is extended over the pole to the lower parts, and the pole is tied to the armpit and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its reduction is completed in whatever way possible to you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of sufficient quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrrh and egg white to the entire muscle, as it is rolled up; and then bind tightly under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be rolled over the plaster, and his hand should be attached to his neck, and it should be left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's diet be light until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for his healing.
And then the diet should be gradually increased after seven or five days and movement exercised; and if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that affects it or another cause, then cautery with three holes should be administered in that place, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the binding are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and hangs and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that his nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are incised or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the back parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to that [area], and treat it until it is healed.
And if during healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially to the front and to the back.  And its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident upon sight or touch, depending on how it is dislocated. And when you align the dislocated hip with its socket, this is clearly evident to you, because the joint is concave and one cannot bend the leg nor touch the thigh with the hand.
And it is necessary that you act quickly and reduce the dislocation before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the back, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and often causes death.
