And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's leg should be extended with two hands, and the leg should be spread out, and the physician's hands should be on and under the hip, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front, then reduce it by bending the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer forceful and very strong extension.
And it is so that two assistants extend the arm, and two other assistants hold that extension so that it is not loosened during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it is wrapped in a long, doubled cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with a strong impulse until it returns [to its place].
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed on a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be tightened with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And then it should be untied; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie its bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well-knit, then you repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and then untie it.
But if gravity occurs during the reduction of the joint, and the patient's disposition is agitated, then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place on the joint  allayments, that is, something very fatty; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bones day and night. And then remove it, and put it in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you rub the joint with a tempered friction, and then repeat the application of the allayment, that is, very fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the bath treatment, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place upon it warm, moist cow dung and bind it tightly; and do this several times, because it is moist and reduces the inflammation.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints. However, speed is essential in its reduction at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table and the assistant extends the hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the belly of the hand; then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table during extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is toward the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be inside-out on the table, so that the physician's hand rests on that prominence of the joint.
And thus, if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then constrict it with a poultice, allowing the inflammation to subside, and leave it.  And do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to succeed in its reduction once several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains crooked and causes no harm to the patient other than that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are pinched or crushed.  In this case, there is no remedy other than to constrict it with a cautery near the injury site, and quickly give it something to support it.
Therefore, once the limb is returned to its place, apply the poultice we have recommended, and let it remain and constrict for five days; then unbind it, and leave it unbound, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some severity occurs, then soothe it with warm water and massage several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated on the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation back with gentle pressure until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it toward the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straight and aligned on that same day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight (or strong/stable).
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform this your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts (of the body).
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death is expected for the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and many times some of their limbs are paralyzed,  either both legs, or both arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is dislocated, because sometimes it is dislocated and sometimes the dislocation is done to four parts.
Therefore, that is dislocated in the back parts and is called yellowness (jaundice -  likely referring to a symptom related to the spinal injury, not literal jaundice).
But the treatment is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice-like symptom) comes from a fever, because then in that case there is neither cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or percussion or similar, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have abbreviated that, whose little excuses the excess of it, by my proximity to my intended exposition, with which I have exposed and formed this from diverse instruments of that with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two sides properly is cured with what I have recounted.
And it is that the patient should be laid on his face on a soft cushion near a wall, and a soft mattress should be placed under him so that his feet are not chafed.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the head end of the cushion near his head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the cushion.
And the attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another attendant should hold the other one in the same manner.
Then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and under his armpits firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is near his head, and tie it to it; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, his buttocks, and around his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is near his feet.
And then each attendant should stretch the bandage to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be moved from their places where they are fixed, but however, they should not be fixed as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. And then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a lathe which is rotated by hand; and you should place in the ground near the patient's head at the back of the buttocks two poles, the length of which is one arm's length, and near their head at the back of the buttocks similarly two poles; and the length between each pole should be one palm, and a hole should be made in each pole through which the lathe passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And there should be another round pole on which the lathe is, and it should be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm including that which is rotated; and in the other two poles it is similar to that.
And then tighten the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole which is near their head, and the ropes that you have tied to their thighs in the lathe which is near their feet.
And then near each lathe let a servant stand who turns the lathe with his hand, and the physician manipulates the spine as we have said.
And then, once the spine is returned and the place is manipulated, then you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of cloth whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is sufficient to cover the area of the curvature, and over the healthy part of the spine.
And then it should be bound with the bandage which we have mentioned, and nourish the patient with a light diet until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines should be administered which soften and smooth, with the administration of the cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is also applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the spinal column, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that is a bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made on it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and that which happens to other joints with small movement and concavity does not happen to these.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without lengthening the leg, and it is in this place, where lengthening is accompanied by a hot abscess, that the head of the femur has already arrived.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwardly, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, yet he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so without limping forwards; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the hip is softened, and the head of the femur is in that place.
But regarding the reduction of these types of dislocation, you should consider that if the dislocation is old and ancient, already set with the limb having adapted to it, and you attempt reduction, and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not a complete cure; and therefore no prognosis should be made in that case.
But in the case where the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care to return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and needs no further treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand from above so that he places his hands under the patient's armpits; then tighten a linen bandage around the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from the inside of the hip to the fork (presumably the groin), or from the back to the back.  (The last part is a little ambiguous;  "dos" might refer to the back, specifically.)
And let the extension of all those (assistants) be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type (of dislocation) that which we shall say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the patient lie supine on his sound side; and then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the ligament upwards from the groin to the highest part of the fork (groin).
And then another strong assistant should take his arms, and grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a strong extension, because that will return it to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is difficult for you and it does not yield to this method of treatment at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet are tied together with a strong, light bandage, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (bandage) be the amount of four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended from the head of a wooden beam, and let him be inside the house; and let his height from the ground be the amount of two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to reduce the head of the thigh, and let another young man suspend the patient, and the other young man, reducing the joint with strong pressure, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the actual reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back as we have recommended in the case of fever; and let the bandage be tightened on the affected leg and on his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the mound placed upon which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that the extension downwards is not pulled.
And then each assistant should extend to his part, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same account on a mound; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the patient's hip, and then grasps that with the other hand, and with that grasping pulls that which is extended downwards to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and he is elevated above the earth; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary to raise him if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips; and if he is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, it is not good.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a cloth on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as necessary — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient straightens his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as necessary — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it tightly so that the hip is not moved in any part; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
Then untie it, and have him delay in walking about for some days until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its dislocations is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can, thereupon; adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then make his knee adhere to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee with strength, and another assistant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light, effortless ease.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with bandages, the use of which in the treatment of the hip was described earlier.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus. (Talus refers to the ankle bone)
The talus is sometimes displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, extending towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is by gentle reduction, and it is that it should be extended easily with the hands and manipulated until it returns [to its place].
But in its treatment when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient lie down straight, and that a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind, at the level of his back.
And then you take with your right hand the foot from its upper part, and with your left hand the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and push the toes of the foot with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have described.
And then push the toes of the foot towards the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two times according to this method, and if you see the foot set correctly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, because it will return [to its proper place].
But if the reduction is forbidden to you by what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tighten a strong rope firmly on the ground, so that it falls between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that the patient is not harmed.
And then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied above the ankle; and then let each attendant pull against the extension of their partner, and let the rope remain between the patient's thighs restraining it, so that their body is not pulled downwards against its extension.
And then the physician sets the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh below, because the dislocation returns easily.
And so when the dislocation is reduced and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and severely injures them.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and worsened, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then you must administer to its suppuration that which we described previously, in another place of treatment and embrocation, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that that which is dislocated be healed with little extension without effort; and this, which is not difficult, is sooner lighter (easier/quicker).
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the foot, then you must make the patient sit with his foot on the ground or on a table, and he should remain thus as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on that which protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is now healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which there are two ends, and then tighten that with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these things happen, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often results.
And therefore, one should not presume to cure it, except for someone skilled in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and who uses in the beginning medicines that only reduce hot abscesses, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for one in whom you see hope of recovery, with alleviation of the disease, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the ailment, before a hot abscess develops.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regime which reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable, using drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and happens in the jagged separated bones, then you should attempt to reunite them, and do in this what we have said in the simple diseases that precede this in those locations.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for that is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
